TOGO
APPRAISAL OF MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
Background
i.        The Government of Togo has asked for the assistance of IDA and
France's FAC to help finance the agricultural development of the Maritime
region, one of the country's most heavily populated regions. The prime
objectives would be to improve and diversify crop production, and to
strengthen the agricultural services, for the benefit of some 20,000
farm families over the five-year period 1976 to 1981. The proposed
project was identified by an RMWA mission in July 1973. The feasibility
study, financed by the UNDP, was carried out by consultants in May-June
1974; FAC financed the preparation of soil maps. A pre-appraisal mission
of Messrs. S. Weed and J. Pelissier visited the country in June-July 1975.
It was followed by an appraisal mission comprising Messrs. Weed, Pelissier
and Kanchanalak (IDA) and Vieilhescaze (consultant) in October-November,
1975, supported by Mr. G. Losson (IDA) on coconuts and Mr. M. Harrison (In-
ternational Institute of Tropical Agriculture) on agricultural research.
The Project Area and Summary Description
ii.       The project area would be the Maritime region in the south of
Togo. It supports 37% of the country's population, while covering only
10% of the total area, and includes an overpopulated zone with depleted
soils.
iii.      The proposed project would be the first phase of an integrated
rural development program for the project area. It would directly benefit
about 20,000 farm families over the five year period of 1976 to 1981. It
would be carried out primarily by the SORAD Maritime, the regional develop-
ment agency, and would involve:
(a) Farm and Crop Development. Providing farmers with
inputs, equipment, and extension and credit ser-
vices to (i) improve and diversify the produc-
tion of the principal upland crops (maize, cas-
sava, groundnuts, cowpeas, and cotton) on some
18,500 ha for 12,000 families; (ii) develop about
3,000 ha of lowland rainfed rice for 6,000 fami-
lies, and provide about 110 power tillers and 75
village rice mills; (iii) develop 25 ha of vegeta-
ble production for 250 families, and rehabilitate
irrigation facilities on 20 ha of existing vegeta-
ble production; and (iv) renovate 600 ha of small-
holder coconut for 2,000 families.



- ii -
(b) Farmer Support Services. (i) Strengthening
SORAD Maritime by providing additional staff,
staff training, buildings and vehicles, ex-
panding seed multiplication, and establishing
an input supply unit; and (ii) establishing
an agency of CNCA to meet project credit require-
ments.
(c) Infrastructure. Constructing or improving
300 km of roads, 15 dispensaries, 20 wells,
2 earth dams, 20 village markets, and 150
village stores.
(d) Studies and Field Trials. (i) Carrying out
an engineering study for the gravity irriga-
tion of 1,000 ha in the lower Zio valley for
rice production; (ii) conducting field trials
on techniques to restore the productivity of
depleted soils, and to test the resistance of
coconut hybrids to Kaincope disease; (iii) estab-
lishing a project monitoring and evaluation unit;
and (iv) providing consultant services to make
investment proposals for the area of North Togo to
be cleared of onchocerciasis.
Field Development
iv.       The project would encourage the adoption of improved farm tech-
niques including the use of selected seed varieties, fertilizers, pest con-
trol and improved crop husbandry by making available to farmers inputs on
credit and extension services.
v.        Upland Crops.  In the savannah, on-farm development would focus
on promoting cotton production in rotation with maize and groundnuts among
3,000 farmers. The main thrust of this subproject, however, would be in the
coastal plateau (8,000 farms) where rainfall is marginal for cotton and the
principal problem is the threat of soil depletion. Farmers would be encour-
aged to emphasize maize during the long rainy season, because the short sea-
son is risky, and to stress rotation with legumes. In the depleted coastal
plateau, the principal problem is that soils have lost most of their produc-
tive potential due to population pressure and continuous cropping. Unfor-
tunately, techniques are not yet available to restore productivity econom-
ically. Thus the project would include field trials of promising techniques
developed at IITA (minimum tillage and mulching) for dissemination during
the last two years of disbursement. Meanwhile, limited improvements would
be sought among 1,000 farmers by stressing legumes, groundnuts and cowpeas
intercropped with fertilized maize during the long rainy season.
vi.       Lowland Rainfed Rice.  The project would provide for the develop-
ment of 3,000 ha of rice to benefit some 6,000 families. SORAD would be
equipped to open up the land on credit by plowing. Growers associations



- iii -
would receive credit for pedal threshers, hand winnowers and small rice
mills. The project would test the use of power tillers and, if the tests
are satisfactory, 100 tillers would be procured beginning the third year
of the project.
vii.      Coconuts.  Renovation program on 600 ha benefitting 2,000 small-
holders would be carried out chiefly by organizing the supply on long-term
credit of hybrid seedlings resistant to the Kaincope disease. The project
would develop a 6 ha seed orchard with technical support by IRHO.
Infrastructure
viii.     The project would strengthen SORAD's engineering unit to execute
the infrastructure part of the project. The construction, improvement and
maintenance of feeder roads would be carried out by SORAD on force account
as the Department of Public Works deals only with primary roads. Contrac-
tors would be hired to construct secondary roads. The wells would be con-
structed by the Ministry of Public Works and upon completion would be turned
over to district authorities as is usual in Togo, as would the village mar-
kets built under contract. The village stores would be built by members of
the growers association who would be provided with materials on medium-term
credit. Dispensaries would be located in agreement with the Ministry of
Hlealth, which would ultimately take over the facilities and ensure their
proper staffing, maintenance and supply.
Organization and Management
ix.       Project Management.  At the outset, the SORAD Maritime would exec-
ute the project in cooperation with CNCA and other specialized Government
services. Government intends to unify all extension services under the
Ministry of Rural Development. IDA would have the opportunity to comment
on any reorganization to minimize disruption of project execution.
x.        Key Staff.  The present director of SORAD, who is a competent
Togolese national, would be the project manager.  He would be assisted by
a technical director, an administrative and financial director, and a chief
of the engineering unit responsible for infrastructure development.
xi.       Extension Services.  Sector chiefs would be in charge of the five
sectors which correspond to the administrative districts of the region, and
would supervise the subsector chiefs. The project would deploy an addi-
tional 20 subsector chiefs and 143 field extension workers, giving an over-
all satisfactory density of five field workers for each subsector chief,
and one extension agent per 150 farmers. Project funds would provide the
extension services with appropriate buildings, 35 motor vehicles, 48 motor-
cycles and 202 bicycles.
xii.      Grower Associations.  The project staff would assist and monitor
the organization of 7-15 participants into grower associations. At a later
stage, these informal groups would federate into cooperatives. Extension,



- iv-
input supply, and credit would be delivered through grower associations.
Ultimately, it is expected that the cooperatives would control input pro-
curement and distribution.
xiii.     Supply of Inputs and Equipment.  To ensure effective input sup-
ply to farmers, a special unit would be staffed and equipped under the
project within SORAD. The long-term objective would be to give profes-
sionally managed grower associations full responsibility for input sup-
ply. The unit would be organized with this objective in mind and, by the
fourth year of the project, a review would be made of the possibilities
of achieving this purpose.
xiv.      Credit Agency.  A credit agency would be established as a branch
of CNCA for the Maritime region at Tsevie, and would work in close liaison
with project management. The head of the credit agency would be a quali-
fied and experienced senior credit specialist, who would supervise four
credit assistants provided and equipped by the project.
xv.       Staffing.  The key posts which remain to be filled are those of
technical director, administrative and financial director, training spe-
cialist, research specialist, chief of the engineering unit and head of
the credit agency. It is anticipated that a competent national would be
available to become the chief of the engineering unit, but expatriates
are expected to be necessary for the other posts. The technical direc-
tion would be assured by an expatriate during the disbursement period,
while other expatriate staff would be required for periods ranging from
2 to 4 years. A research specialist would be assigned to conduct field
trials on depleted soils.
xvi.      Training.  As staff and farmer training is an important aspect
of the project, funds would be provided for the construction and operation
of a training center at Tsevie. The program would be organized by a train-
ing specialist appointed under the project. Funds would also be allocated
for specialized training of technical personnel at the International Insti-
tute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) in Nigeria.
xvii.     Consultants would be engaged to coordinate a gravity irrigation
engineering study for the devèlopment of 1,000 ha for one cycle cropping
with rice, and give technical support for field trials on the resistance
of coconuts to the Kaincope disease and on the rehabilitation of depleted
soils. Consultants would also be engaged to give support to the project
monitoring and evaluation unit, and to carry out feasibility studies on
the development of the area benefiting from onchocerciasis eradication.
Project Costs and Financing
xviii.    The estimated cost of the project is US$15.7 million or US$13.9
million net of taxes and duties (which are about 11% of total costs). The
foreign exchange component would be US$9.3 million, or 67% of the total net
of taxes. Physical contingencies and expected price increases would amount
to 35% of base cost estimates, which are as of April 1976.



v 
xix.      The IDA credit of US$9.5 million would be made to Government at
standard terms. FAC has agreed to cofinance the project on a pari passu
basis through a grant of FF 10 million (US$2.2 million). The remainder
of project costs would be financed by Government and farmers.
xx.       Except for the costs of rice land preparation and water for vege-
table growing, which would be fully charged to beneficiaries and represent
a minor portion of project cost, other costs to be incurred by SORAD would
be for services and investments which are normally financed out of the gen-
eral budget and are not directly recoverable from users. Therefore CFAF
1,940 million (US$8.6 million) would be passed on to SORAD as a grant.
CNCA's own resources are limited. Consequently, the project funds to fi-
nance the operating costs of the credit agency and the revolving fund for
the purchase of seasonal inputs (CFAF 291 million or US$1.3 million) would
be a Government contribution to CNCA. The funds required for the medium-
term and long-term credit (CFAF 140 million or US$0.6 million) would be
on-lent to CNCA at 5.5% (the Central Bank's rediscount rate for medium
term lending) for a term of 15 years including seven years of grace.
Credit Arrangements
xxi.      Credit to farmers would bear an interest rate of 8% and would
require guarantee fees totalling 3%. Medium-term credit would be for a term
of four years and long-term credit for coconut rehabilitation would be for 12
years, including a grace period of seven years.
Procurement
xxii.    Contracts for the supply of goods other than rock phosphate, which
are estimated to cost US$50,000 or more each, would be awarded on the basis
of international competitive bidding in accordance with IDA guidelines (total
cost about US$3.4 million). Domestically manufactured goods would be allowed
a preference of up to 15 percent or the level of applicable duty, whichever is
less, when comparing domestic bids with those of foreign manufacturers. Supply
contracts valued at less than US$50,000 (the number of which would be minimized
by bulking procurement and which would total about US$200,000) would be let
after competitive bidding in accordance with local procedures, as would
contracts for the construction of 90 km of secondary roads (total cost about
US$1.2 million) and of project buildings, dispensaries and rural markets
(total cost about US$0.9 million) which would be individually small and widely
dispersed. IDA staff have reviewed the local procedures, which are acceptable;
there would be adequate competition. Rock phosphate, costing about US$0.1
million, would be procured at reasonable prices from Government mines. Small
items costing less than US$10,000 would be awarded after solicitation of
quotations from at least three suppliers (US$100,000). Inputs purchased
directly by farmers, would be obtained through local shopping (US$0.5 million).
SORAD, which would be adequately equipped under the project, would construct
or improve about 210 km of feeder roads and build two earth dams on force
account for an estimated total cost of US$1.7 million. The project well
component (costing about US$1.8 million spread over five years), would be
carried out on force account by the well brigade of the Ministry of Public



- vi -
Works which is competent and sufficiently equipped. Consultant services and
expatriate staff (totaling about US$2.6 million) would be obtained according
to IDA's normal procedures. Costs for local personnel and other operating
costs, which do not lend themselves to competitive bidding, amount to about
US$3.2 million.
Disbursements
xxiii.    The IDA credit would be disbursed to cover:
(a)  80% of foreign expenditures or 60% of total expenditures
for incremental fertilizer, pesticides and imported seeds,
and for equipment for farmer groups (US$0.77 million);
(b)  80% of foreign expenditures or 60% of total expenditures for
building construction; the procurement of equipment, vehicles
and office furnishings; incremental staff salaries and train-
ing grants; and other incremental costs of SORAD and CNCA
(US$3.28 million);
(c) 60% of costs of infrastructure construction (US$2.25 million);
(d) 100% of the costs of consultant services for the planning of de-
velopment for areas to be freed from onchocerciasis (US$0.3 million);
(e) 80% of the costs of project monitoring and evaluation, and of other
studies and consultant services (US$0.52 million).
The remaining funds would cover a contingency reserve of US$2.38 million.
Marketing and Prices
xxiv.     Because of the net food deficit of the country, the demand is strong
for foodgrains and groundnuts, which are sold at attractive prices on local
markets. The export outlook for cotton is good and prices are adequate, mar-
keting arrangements are satisfactory. The demand and incentives exist, there-
fore, for increased production such as planned under the project.
Benefits and Justification
xxv.      The project's direct benefits, from the increased output of maize,
yam, groundnuts, cowpeas, cotton, rice and vegetables would accrue to some
20,000 families who, consequently, would considerably increase their pres-
ently low farm incomes.  Net family incomes from farming would increase from
about US$80 to about US$170 in the poorest areas, from US$190 to US$350 in
most cases, and by up to US$460 for vegetables. The project would also have
important non-quantifiable social, institutional and ecological benefits.
Improved health services would benefit about 150,000 people, and water sup-
plies about 44,000. At full development, the project would provide additional
seasonal agricultural employment totaling about 1.4 million man-days per



- vii -
year. The project would have a major institution building impact, partic-
ularly on SORAD Maritime and CNCA, and would help create conditions for
replicating rural development projects in the project area and the country
at large. The project is also intended to set the stage to prevent further
soil depletion in the coastal plateau area and to reverse soil depletion
where it has already occurred, in one of the poorest areas of the country.
xxvi.     The economic rate of return of the project would be 37% over 15
years (30 years for coconuts). Sensitivity analysis shows that the dominant
parameters are the assumptions on yields and prices. Separate rates of
return calculated for the six production components range from 13% for coco-
nuts to 52% for vegetables.
Risks
xxvii.    The project would represent the first major involvement of IDA in
up-land arable crop production in a bi-modal rainfall area of West Africa.
Indeed the project would be the largest externally financed project in such
an area in Western Africa. Throughout most of the region the principal crops
in these areas are tree crops - cocoa, coffee, oil palm and rubber - and agri-
cultural research has concentrated on the requirements of these largely ex-
port tree crops. The food crops of the area have generally been neglected
by researchers and even where technology packages have been developed, the
period and scale of testing at the farmer level has been limited. Therefore
the assumptions concerning yield increments at farm level may be subject to
substantial error and, as a consequence, so may the assumptions regarding
farmer response to innovations promoted under the project. Further, although
in these areas total rainfall and the duration of the rainy season appear sub-
stantially better than in areas further north, where a single peak rainfall
occurs and the rainy season is much shorter, the benefit of such a rainfall
regime may be more illusory than real. This is because the second rainy
season is often too short to guarantee profitable crops; and crops grown in
the area during the wet season often have to be harvested and stored under
very adverse wet and humid conditions. Given these circumstances, the tech-
nology base for the project generally is less firm than for other arable crop
projects in Western Africa and although the situation is improving, in partic-
ular, because of the establishment of the International Institute for Tropical
Agriculture at Ibadan, Nigeria, which was specifically created to deal with
the problems of this ecological zone, the risks involved in the project must
be considered substantial. Nonetheless, the critical situation in the proj-
ect area, described in the report, demands that the risk be taken.
Recommendation
xxviii.   With the assurances and conditions set out in Chapter IX, the
project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$9.5 million.






I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Togo has asked for the assistance of IDA and
France's FAC to help finance the agricultural development of the Maritime
region, one of the country's most heavily populated regions. The prime
objectives would be to improve and diversify crop production, and to
strengthen the agricultural services, for the benefit of some 20,000
farm families.
1.02      The proposed project was identified by an RMWA mission in July
1973. The feasibility study, financed by the UNDP, was carried out by
consultants in May-June 1974; FAC financed the preparation of soil maps.
1.03      A pre-appraisal mission of Messrs. S. Weed and J. Pelissier vis-
ited the country in June-July 1975. It was followed by an appraisal mission
comprising Messrs. Weed, Pelissier and Kanchanalak (IDA) and Vieilhescaze
(consultant) in October-November, 1975, supported by Mr. G. Losson (IDA)
on coconuts and Mr. M. Harrison (International Institute of Tropical Agri-
culture) on agricultural research.
1.04      The Bank has made three credits to Togo:  Credit 131-TO (US$3.7
million) in 1968, and Credit 450-TO (US$8.7 million) in 1973 for road main-
tenance; and Credit 503-TO (US$6.0 million), signed in June 1974 for the
improvement of smallholder cocoa and coffee production, which is progress-
ing satisfactorily.
Il. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Togo is located on the Gulf of çuinea and bounded by Ghana, Benin
and Upper Volta; it covers some 56,000 km . Population is about 2.1 million
and growing at about 2.7% per year.
2.02      From 1960-73, Togo experienced one of the highest growth rates in
per capita income in Africa, averaging more than 4% per year. The sustained
expansion in Togo's economy through 1970 was largely due to a vigorous ex-
pansion of external commerce spurred by favorable market conditions for two
of the country's principal exports, cocoa and coffee, and by continued devel-
opment of Togo's position as an important regional commercial entrepot. In
the early 1970's, economic growth slowed down. Adverse weather conditions,
particularly in the period 1971-73, affected agricultural production; a size-
able reduction in border trade coupled with a reversal of market conditions
for Togo's main exports led to a deterioration of the balance of payments.
From 1971 to 1975, however, the country's economic development was sustained
primarily by the expansion of the industrial and mining sector (and to a
lesser extent the service sector) with the phosphate boom in 1974 trigger-
ing a resurgence in economic growth. It is tentatively estimated that per
capita income reached US$250 in 1975, although rural incomes average only
about 1/3 of that amount. Togo's longer-term outlook is favorable with



prospects for GDP growth during the next decade of about 6 percent per
year, based on further diversification of the economy and exploitation of
natural resources, and for further improvement in income distribution and
regional development.
B. The Agricultural Sector
General
2.03      Agriculture is the most important sector of the economy, employing
about 80% of the active population. It accounts for about 30% of both GDP and
export earnings.
2.04      During the first plan, agricultural production in real terms in-
creased by an estimated 3-4% per year. However, the second plan (1971-75)
goal of 6.5% annual growth in constant prices apparently was not met, although
poor statistics make a full assessment of the situation difficult. Estimates
for food crop production (essentially yams, cassava, maize and sorghum) are
particularly weak. It is clear however that an adequate supply of domestic
foodstuffs is likely to become increasingly a problem; in 1973 Togo imported
about 20,000 ton of foodgrain (wheat, rice and maize).
2.05      The value of agricultural exports has tended to stagnate, as produc-
tion of all major crops has declined in recent years. Cocoa and coffee have
suffered from lack of new investment and of proper maintenance due to the
low levels of producer prices in the early 1960's. Recent price increases,
and the establishment of the coffee-cocoa renovation agency (SRCC) to promote
rehabilitation and new planting, should help reverse this trend. Cotton pro-
duction also suffered from a lack of adequate price incentives and technical
support although an ambitious cotton development program is under prepara-
tion. Copra production has been sharply decreased by the ravages of the
Kaincope disease and exports have ceased, while the production of groundnuts
has been largely diverted to local consumption because of low official prices.
Regional Characteristics
2.06      The northernmost Savannah region is characterized by an irregular
and short-duration fainfall pattern (1,100 mm). The overall population den-
sity is about 30/km , but while the Oti Valley is virtually empty becaus9 of
onchocerciasis, other parts of the region are overpopulated (up to 90/km ).
Principal crops are millet/sorghum, groundnuts, and recently, rice; the area
has moderate prospects for livestock development.
2.07      The Kara region, a mountainous region, has adequate rainfall
(1,400 mm) but low agricultural potential due to overpopulation and the  2
resultant depletion of soil fertility. Population density averages 53/km
but reaches almost 300/km in some areas, while about one fourth of the
region is unpopulated due to onchocerciasis. Migration to the south, both
seasonal (about 20% of the male population) and permanent, is already signi-
ficant and will have to continue if the problems of overpopulation in the
region are to be solved.



- 3 -
2.08 2    The Central region is the least populated region with only about
19/km . Precipitation, which averages about 1,300 mm in a single season, and
generally adequate soils can support crops such as cotton, groundnuts, yams,
maize, and pulses. However, the absence of an easily accessible aquifer to
supply drinking water is a major constraint to settlement and agricultural
development.
2.09      The Plateau region, with average rainfall of 1,200-1,6002mm (bi-
modal), generally good soils and population density of about 30/km , has
the best agricultural potential. Cocoa and coffee dominate the wetter, moun-
tainous area to the west, while the eastern part of the region is suited for
integrated cotton/food crop development and intensive maize cultivation.
2.10      The Maritime region in the south would be the project area.  Its
description is outlined in paras 3.01-3.10 and detailed in Annex 3.
Institutional Framework
2.11      Responsibility for the Agricultural Sector is shared by two min-
istries which replaced the Ministry of Rural Economy in early 1975. The
Ministry of Rural Development (MDR) is essentially concerned with policy
formulation and program coordination for production activities. The Ministry
of Rural Equipment (MER) is concerned primarily with infrastructure develop-
ment such as irrigation works and rural roads.
2.12      The execution of development projects in the sector is entrusted
to autonomous public corporations which are either regionally or product
oriented. The five SORADS (Societes Regionales d'Amenagement et de Devel-
oppement), one for each region listed in para 2.06-2.10, were established
in 1966-67 with responsibility for all development activities. A major
problem of the SORADS has been the lack of clearly defined programs (Annex
1). The proposed project would for the first time constitute a well-defined,
comprehensive program for the SORAD Maritime. Another problem has been gov-
ernment's requirement that the SORADS attempt to be self-financing primarily
through commercial activities. In retrospect this objective has proven to
be unsound, as the SORADS' main activities (extension, training) are of a
type that are normally financed from Government budgets, and it is difficult
to recover these costs except under very exceptional circumstances. Commer-
cial activities have recently been taken over by specialized agencies: SRCC
for coffee and cocoa, SONAPH for oilpalm, TOGOFRUIT for fruits, SOTOCO for
cotton, TOGOGRAIN for foodgrains. The responsibilities of these agencies
range from the supply of inputs through the provision of extension services
to primary marketing and processing. In many instances, duplication of ex-
tension efforts has resulted between the specialized agencies and the SORADS.
Government is now considering the regrouping of all extension activities
under a unified service, organized along regional lines, attached to the
MDR. Technical support, training, marketing and processing for specific
crops would still be supplied by the specialized agencies (already report-
ing to MDR). The SORADS, which have been assigned to the MER, would ulti-
mately concentrate on rural engineering and infrastructure activities.
Although Government has no timetable as yet for this reorganization, its
objectives are sound.



- 4 -
2.13      The marketing of agricultural exports is the responsibility of
OPAT (Office des Produits Agricoles Togolais). Its main function is the
stabilization of prices for export crops; it is also involved in the financing
and overall supervision of the marketing of these crops. OPAT purchases are
executed through a network of registered buyers at official prices. Such
prices normally allow OPAT a sizable export profit margin and thus, as is
customary in French-speaking West African countries, OPAT's profits have been
used to finance general development, but only some 6% for agriculture.
2.14      The Caisse Nationale de Credit Agricole -- CNCA (Annex 2) was
established in 1967 to provide credit to the agricultural sector. Most
of CNCA's credits to farmers have been channelled through the specialized
corporations or the SORADS. Inadequate credit policy and weak management
resulted in a high percentage of defaults and arrears; but a new manage-
ment (installed in 1974) should improve operations considerably. Non-
agricultural loans have now been stopped. Direct arrangements with
farmer groups, being successfully tested under the cocoa-coffee project
financed by IDA (Credit 503-TO) and to be encouraged under the proposed
project, should improve loan recovery. A report on CNCA's reorganization
and credit administration, prepared in 1975 under the IDA Credit, was re-
ceived favorably by Government, and CNCA has begun to implement the recom-
mendations which are detailed in Annex 2.
2.15      Research is carried out by French commodity oriented institutes
(food crops, coffee-cocoa, oil crops, fiber crops, animal husbandry). Re-
cently, Government created a multidisciplinary research institute (Insti-
tut Polyvalent de Recherches) with headquarters at Cacavelli near Lome.
This new agency has presently a limited staff and carries out a limited
program.
Subsidies
2.16      To promote the use of inputs as part of improved farming prac-
tices, Government has been subsidizing fertilizers and pesticides. The
current fertilizer cost to the farmers is CFAF 15/kg, which means a sub-
sidy of 50% to 70% of costs. It is almost universally argued in West
Africa that subsidies are necessary to introduce the use of modern in-
puts to farmers. While this argument has merit, it is also true that
once farmers become acquainted with the use of inputs, subsidies may
lead to their misuse and become a burden on public finances. A policy
of gradual reduction of the level of subsidies is therefore advisable,
and this would be facilitated by the expected decrease in world fertil-
izer prices. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from Gov-
ernment that it would consult annually with IDA on prices to be paid by
farmers for fertilizers and pesticides, with a view to progressively
eliminating the subsidy element.



- 5 -
C. Government Sectoral Objectives and Strategies
2.17      The third development plan (1976-80), now in the final stages of
preparation, calls for substantial improvements in agricultural performance
both in industrial and food crops.
2.18      In the case of industrial crops, government's objectives are to
restore traditional exports, and to diversify into new products. A major
program for coffee and cocoa, which account for about 90% of agricultural
exports, was begun in 1974 with IDA assistance (Credit 503-TO), and should
begin yielding results by the end of the decade. A five-year cotton program
aimed at increasing production from 12,000 to 42,000 tons has been submitted
to the Bank Group for consideration but may require a scaling down of these
objectives. There is some scope for rehabilitating traditional copra produc-
tion, but marginal ecological conditions rule out any significant development
of coconuts. Other possibilities, including sugar cane, tobacco and cashew
nuts, are being studied by Government.
2.19      In the case of food crops, Government hopes not only to slow down
imports but eventually to reach self-sufficiency. This objective should be
attainable. A change in the mix of food crops is predicted in the plan. The
production of traditional food grains (maize, millet, sorghum) is expected to
increase but that of tubers (yams and cassava) would lag behind population
growth, because of changes in demand that reflect changes in dietary habits
associated with urbanization. An increase is expected in the production of
pulses and groundnuts. Rice production, and its share of total foodgrain
production, would about double.
2.20      The principal sources of agricultural growth seen in the plan would
be both improvement of yields and expansion of cropped areas. Area increases
are foreseen as being achieved through double-cropping, intercropping or de-
creasing fallow. But substantial efforts will have to be made to bring new
land into production. Some of this land will be found in areas to be freed
of onchocerciasis under the ongoing Regional program; preparation of proj-
ects for the development of these areas is being actively pursued and would
be supported under the proposed project (para 4.26). It is also envisaged
that additional output would come from water resource development, which
would be given priority. Water for human and animal consumption -- crucial
to opening up areas in Central Region and the northern part of Maritime re-
gion -- would be developed through construction of wells and small water-
retention dams. Government also envisages relatively ambitious irrigation
schemes on the Zio, Haho, Mono, and Oti rivers, but there is scope for sim-
pler, less expensive approaches, including the development of lowland areas
for rainfed rice and other crops, and low cost gravity irrigation for which
good potential exists.



- 6 -
III. THE PROJECT AREA
3.01      The project area would be the eniire Maritime region in the south
of Togo (Map 11885). The region, 6,100 km in area (about 10% of the coun-
try), lies on the coast of the Bight of Benin; it includes the capital city
of Lome. It has a tropical climate, with a bimodal rainfall ranging from 800
to 1,100 mm. It is divided into the ecological zones outlined below (details
in Annex 3).
3.02      The savannah in the north has the highest rainfall and thus sup-
ports two crops annually; it is a gently rolling plateau with soils of lim-
ited fertility but easy to work and suitable for a variety of crops: maize,
cotton, groundnuts, cassava, yam. It is sparsely populated, and has a good
potential for development of agriculture and settlement, though lacking access
roads and adequate village water supply. Some areas are subject to river-
blindness.
3.03      The coastal plateau of the center can produce two crops, but the
low rainfall of the second rainy season reduces the potential for productive
second cropping. It has a flat topography, deep, red and well-structured
soils with high productivity, suitable for maize, cassava and groundnuts;
fallowing has largely disappeared because of population pressure. Mixed
cropping of maize and cassava prevails.
3.04      The depleted coastal plateau of the southeast has marginal rain-
fall, with cropping during the second season risky. High population and
overcropping have caused severe fertility depletion and erosion of soils,
which further limit the agricultural potential. Maize and cassava dominate.
3.05      In the coastal sands, between the sea and the lagoons, the only
cash crop is coconuts although large scale commercial production is not
practicable because of low rainfall.
3.06      The Lama or Bado depression, crossing the coastal plateau, is made
up of heavy, poorly drained soils but nevertheless has a good potential for
rice. Some areas need access roads.
3.07      The river valleys (Zio, Haho and Mono) have a good potential for
rice and irrigated crops. In the Zio and Haho valleys the river flow with-
out regulation limits the irrigation potential mostly to single cropping.
The Mono river offers possibilities for double cropping. Rainfed rice, a
recent introduction in the western tributary valleys, can be expanded.
3.08      The population of the region is about 806,000, or some 37% of the
national total. The annual rate of increase averages 3.1% (2.7% in rural
areas, 4.5% in the ciiies). While northern parts have a population density
of no more than 25/km , the s9uthern part of the coastal plateau supports
a rural density of 160-200 km , causing a heavy strain on very restricted
soil resources.



3.09      Farm Size and Tenure.  The average farm family has 8 people, of
which 5 are active. About half the farms cover less than 1 ha, one third
1-2 ha, and only 3% have more than 4 ha. Farm size increases from the
southern part of the Coastal plateau to the savannah of the north. The
area has a complex land tenure system, with communal ownership being prog-
ressively replaced by individual ownership. In the overpopulated areas, up
to one fourth of the land is managed by tenant farmers. This system would
not affect the project which would have limited involvement in the overpop-
ulated zones.
3.10      Present Problems.  The main problems encountered in the project
area are: (a) net food deficit; (b) overpopulation, soil depletion, and
low income particularly in the Southeast; (c) Kaincope disease 1/, which
has drastically cut coconut production, the only source of cash income on
the coast; (d) limited coverage of support services to farmers; and (e)
insufficient infrastructure (chiefly roads and village water supply) in
the north.
3.11      The Oti valley in the Savannah region outside the project area (see
insert Map 11886) would be the object of feasibility studies (para 4.26).
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Description
4.01      The proposed project would be the first phase of an integrated
rural development program of the Maritime region. The project would cover
the entire region, but areas of concentration would be selected to benefit
about 20,000 farm families over the five year period of 1976 to 1981. It
would be carried out primarily by the SORAD Maritime and would involve:
(a) Farm and Crop Development. Providing farmers with inputs,
equipment, and extension and credit services to (i) improve
and diversify the production of the principal upland crops
(maize, cassava, groundnuts, cowpeas, and cotton) on some
18,500 ha for 12,000 families; (ii) develop about 3,000 ha
of lowland rainfed rice for 6,000 families, and provide about
100 power tillers and 75 village rice mills; (iii) develop 25
ha of vegetable production for 250 families, and rehabilitate
irrigation facilities on 20 ha of existing vegetable produc-
tion; and (iv) renovate 600 ha of smallholder coconut for
2,000 families.
1/   Apparently caused by a mycoplasm (a parasitic microorganism), resulting
in dropping of the coconuts, followed by a progressive drying up of the
leaves, and the death of the trees.



-8-
(b)  Farmer Support Services.  (i) Strengthening SORAD Maritime
by providing additional staff, staff training, buildings
and vehicles, expanding seed multiplication, and establish-
ing an input supply unit; and (ii) establishing an agency of
CNCA to meet project credit requirements.
(c)  Infrastructure.  Constructing or improving 300 km of roads,
15 dispensaries, 20 wells, 2 earth dams, 20 village markets,
and 150 village stores.
(d)  Studies and Field Trials.  (i) Carrying out an engineering
study for the gravity irrigation of 1,000 ha in the lower
Zio valley for rice production; (ii) conducting field
trials on techniques to restore the productivity of
depleted soils, and to test the resistance of coconut
hybrids to Kaincope disease; (iii) establishing a project
monitoring and evaluation unit; and (iv) providing con-
sultant services to make investment proposals for the
area of North Togo to be cleared of onchocerciasis.
B. Detailed Features
Field Development
4.02      Upland Crops.  The project would expand the adoption of improved
farm techniques already introduced by SORAD, and including use of seed of
improved varieties, fertilization, pest control and improved crop husbandry,
by making available to farmers inputs on credit (para 6.09) and extension
services. Technical details and typical farm budgets are respectively at
Annexes 4 and 5; and the phasing of field development is at Annex 6. Tech-
nical recommendations would be tailored to fit the major farm types and the
ecology of the main zones of the project area as described in the following
paragraphs.
4.03      In the savannah, on-farm development would focus on promoting
cotton production--following techniques successfully introduced by SOTOCO--
in rotation with maize and groundnuts, among some 3,000 farmers. Partici-
pants would be provided with cotton seed variety Allen 333, which is the
best adapted variety available, and with 14-21-21 fertilizer to be applied
at a rate of 150 kg/ha. This is in line with current recommendations in
neighboring countries. They would also be provided with endosulphan or pre-
prothion to make 6 annual pest control applications. Farmers would also
be provided with ATC x Niaouli maize hybrid seed which they would plant,
after seed dressing, to achieve a plant stand of 16,000 to 40,000 per ha,
depending on intercropping. Fertilization with 30-30-30 kg/ha NPK (as rec-
ommended by IRAT and subject to adjustments depending on the results of fur-
ther trials) would be restricted to long-season maize that does not follow
cotton or bush fallow. Farmers would be provided with short cycle ground-
nuts seed of the 55-437 variety and seed dressing. A desirable stand would



- 9 -
be 120,000 to 160,000 per ha depending on intercropping. With these im-
provements, farmers are expected to reach yields of 900 kg/ha of seed cot-
ton, 1,600 kg/ha of shelled maize, and 1,000 kg/ha of unshelled groundnuts.
Maize shellers would be made available to grower associations, see para 4.12,
on credit.
4.04      The main thrust of the project would be in the coastal plateau
(8,000 farms), where productivity is high per unit area for foodcrops, and
SORAD has already been promoting improved practices, but the rainfall is
marginal for cotton, and the principal problem is the threat of soil deple-
tion. Farmers would be encouraged to make judicious use of fertilizer, to
emphasize maize during the long season because the short season is risky,
and to stress rotations with legumes (groundnuts and cowpeas) which can add
nitrogen to the soil. Fertilization (30-30-30 kg/ha of NPK) would be recom-
mended for maize. Varieties and crop husbandry practices, and expected yields
would be the same for maize and groundnuts as in the savannah. Cowpeas would
receive two insecticide applications and the crop would be stored in sealed
plastic bags.
4.05      In the depleted coastal plateau the principal problem is that soils
have lost most of their productive potential due to population pressure and
continuous cropping (para 3.04). As a result yields average a mere 400 kg/ha
each for maize and groundnuts. Unfortunately techniques are not yet available
to restore productivity and improve farm incomes. Attempts have been made by
SORAD to rehabilitate depleted soils, essentially by plowing crop residues,
but the results have been disappointing because of high costs. On the other
hand very promising results have been achieved on humid tropical soils at the
International Institute of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) with minimum tillage
and mulching. Thus the project would field test those techniques at the farm
level (para 4.23 and Annex 7) and promote their extension, if proven to be
viable, to project farmers during the last two project years. Meanwhile
limited improvements would be sought by stressing legumes (groundnut and
cowpeas) in the rotation with maize during the long season and by applying
to maize the improved practices described in para 4.04. The project would
make available inputs and technical support to allow 1,000 farmers to par-
ticipate in the program.
4.06      Lowland Rainfed Rice.  The project would provide for the develop-
ment of some 3,000 ha of rainfed rice on valley land for the benefit of
about 6,000 families, building on previous successful experience of SORAD.
Trained SORAD staff would identify suitable terrain, select farmers, or-
ganize them in grower associations (para 4.12), and supply them with tech-
nical advice. SORAD would be provided with five 80 HP tractors and equip-
ment to initially open up the land by plowing, after bush clearance by the
growers themselves. Farmers would repay the full cost of this operation on
credit. Because of soil compaction and the density of the grass cover,mech-
anical plowing is the only practical method to open up the land. Farmers
would be provided with the Ainachen variety, seed dressing and fertilizer
(80 kg/ha of rock phosphate and 100 kg/ha of urea). Growers would also be
provided with seed and seed dressing for a second crop with cowpeas. Grower
associations would receive credit for pedal threshers, hand winnowers, and
rice mills (1 ton per day). These inexpensive mills, which have been suc-
cessfully introduced in the area, are in great demand.



- 10 -
4.07      In the initial stages of rice development, it is assumed that
the majority of farmers would limit their participation to 0.5 ha, w-hich
would be in addition to their traditional activities. Under these condi-
tions, no labor constraints are foreseen and, after initial plowing by the
project, land preparation would be done by hand. However, as the farmers
place more emphasis on rice growing in their overall activities, land prep-
aration could face labor bottlenecks. Consequently, SORAD would test ox
drawn cultivation as a possible means of increasing labor productivity. As
discussed in Annex 4, the use of power tillers would also be tested under
the project. Funds would be allocated for the purchase during PY 1 of 10
power tillers which would be made available, under appropriate terms, to a few
progressive grower associations or selected individuals. SORAD would submit
to IDA a report on the performance the power tillers at the end of PY 2. If
the tests are judged satisfactory by SORAD and by IDA, credit funds would be
released for the procurement and sale of about 100 additional tillers to
growers starting in PY 3. During negotiations, Government agreed to (a)
follow the proposed sequence for the deployment of power tillers, and (b)
cause SORAD to maintain an adequate stock of spare parts of such power tillers
as are purchased.
4.08      Vegetables.  Small vegetable operations are scattered through-
out the project area, under hand irrigation (Lome area), rainfed, or under
flood recession management. There is scope to improve vegetable production,
through the promotion of productive varieties, more intensive and comprehen-
sive fertilizer use, pest control, and hand irrigation (Annex 4). Therefore
the project would provide extension and supply services to some 250 vegeta-
ble growers throughout the project area, and would set up two small tech-
nical support centers, one near Lome and another in the Zio valley, for
variety testing, field trials and demonstration, and training of exten-
sion personnel.
4.09      For several years, SORAD has operated a vegetable center at Agoueve,
some 15 km north of Lome (Map 11885). This center serves as a vegetable
testing and demonstration station and provides irrigation facilities for some
80 growers cultivating 0.2 ha each. The basic infrastructure is sound;
however replacement of the existing diesel powered pumps is needed. The
project would provide for their replacement by electric pumps which are less
expensive to operate and maintain. Details of the rehabilitation program are
at Annex 8. In view of the relatively high profitability of vegetable growing
(see farm budget, Annex 5), full cost of the investment and operation would be
recovered from the growers through water charges. Up to now SORAD has not been
fully successful in recovering water charges from growers, and a condition of
disbursement against the purchase of new pumps would be that SORAD had initia-
ted an effective system of collecting water charges.
4.10      Coconuts.  The only promising way to cope with Kaincope disease
appears to be the utilization of resistant coconut breeds, esseritially
hybrids of local Talls with imported Dwarfs, such as have been developed
by the French Oil Crop Institute (IRHO). Although there is still some un-
certainty as to the exact degree of resistance of these hybrids, there is
enough evidence on their good performance to recommend their adoption at



- il -
least for small scale plantings (Annex 9 - Appendix 1).  Thus, a renova-
tion program on 600 ha benefitting 2,000 smallholders would be carried
out by organizing the supply on long term credit of hybrid seedlings, fer-
tilizers and pesticides, and providing extension services. To ensure the
supply of hybrid seedlings, the project would develop a 6-ha seed garden
out of existing dwarf plantations, using imported pollen of Tall trees, and
a 2-ha nursery for the production of hybrid seedlings under irrigation, with
technical support by IRHO (para 4.24). Seed production would start in about
1978.
Support Services to Farmers
4.11      Strengthening of SORAD Maritime.  To cope with the project workload,
the staff of SORAD would be strengthened at both management (para 5.07) and
field levels (para 5.05). The project would also finance the expansion of
SORAD headquarters, the building of a training center, the purchase of 35
vehicles for management staff, and 48 motorcycles and 202 bicycles for
extension workers (Annex 1).
4.12      Grower Associations.  SORAD has relied on grower associations as
an effective means of delivering support services, basically input supply
and credit to farmers. This policy is sound and would be encouraged under
the project. Initially, farmers would be organized informally into 7 to
15-member associations which would be closely assisted and monitored by the
field extension staff. At a later stage it is expected that associations
would federate at the village level to form cooperatives. The associations
would own and operate equipment such as maize shellers, paddy threshers and
winnowers and rice mills, that would be too expensive to be purchased by
individuals. The associations would be eligible to obtain medium term cred-
it for these items (para 6.09). The project would provide training to asso-
ciation leaders.
4.13      Extension Services.  Field staff in the project area, total 122 (70
SORAD and 52 SOTOCO), and can only give skeletal extension coverage to the
area's 70,000 farmers; the ratio is one extension worker for more than 500
farmers. The project would deploy an additional 143 field extension workers
and aim at a ratio of one worker to 150 farmers for upland crops, one to 100
for rice and coconuts, and one to 40 for vegetables. This would correspond
to a ratio of one worker to about 15 grower associations for upland crops;
this is a high overall ratio, but during any particular year only the newly
created associations would require close attention. In addition to providing
farmers with agronomic support, extension workers would help farmers and
grower associations to prepare credit applications (para 6.09), and collate
orders for inputs (para 4.15).
4.14      Training.  Basic agricultural training for middle level staff is
available at the agricultural institute at Tove which is located well out-
side the project area. However, the training of lower level field personnel
is normally the responsibility of the individual agencies such as SORAD. As
staff and farmer training would be an important aspect of the project, funds
would be provided for the construction and operation of a training center at



- 12 -
Tsevie which is centrally located (Map 11885). The program would be organized
by a training specialist appointed under the project (see Annex 13 for terms
of reference) with technical expertise being drawn from crop and other special-
ists from within or outside the project. The training division would also be
responsible for the development and production of pamphlets and posters in the
vernacular, for distribution by the extension agents. The project would pro-
vide funds for crop production training (sessions of about 6 months correspond-
ing to a cropping cycle) of technical personnel by the International Institute
of Tropical Agriculture (IITA) in Nigeria. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained that the training specialist would prepare a comprehensive
training program, for discussion with IDA, before December 31, 1976.
4.15      Supply of Inputs and Equipment.  Traditionally, the inputs and
equipment required for agricultural development have been supplied through
extension services such as SORAD.  However, these services have been poorly
organized and as a result there has been recurring difficulty in obtaining the
necessary inputs on time and in adequate quantity. As it is crucial to avoid
these difficulties, an input supply unit, attached to SORAD, would be created
with responsibility for (a) determining input needs on the basis of estimates
collected by the extension services; (b) procuring the supplies on credit
(para 6.09); and (c) organizing their distribution. Individual orders would
be bulked at the association level, and the associations would be responsible
for input distribution among members. The long term objective would be to
enable the grower associations to assume full responsibility for input supply
on a commercial basis, with the help of professional management. To this end
the project's supply unit would maintain accounts in sufficient detail to
identify distribution costs (para 6.08), and by June 30, 1980 a review would
be made of the operations of the input supply unit, of its prospects for
operating on a commercial basis, and of the possibilities of eventual control
of input supply activities by cooperatives. These arrangements were confirmed
during negotiations.
4.16      Seed Multiplication (Annex 7).  SORAD would continue to have re-
sponsibility for seed multiplication in the project area, partly under con-
tract with selected growers. Thus far SORAD has been able to assure a lim-
ited supply of hybrid maize and rice seed using foundation seed supplied by
IRAT. Existing arrangements would be inadequate to meet the substantial
demand generated by the project, and SORAD would be provided with funds to
acquire appropriate equipment and storage facilities. Furthermore, a full-
time seed production officer would be appointed and assurances were received
from Government in this respect. In accordance with ongoing arrangements, the
production of foundation seed would be carried out by IRAT at the Tsevie
station, where the project would build and equip a seed testing laboratory,
and provide for a seed technician.
Infrastructure (Annex 10)
4.17      Roads.  The lack of roads and the poor maintenance of existing
roads especially in the north of the project area hinder crop extraction,
input delivery and the development of underpopulated areas. The project



- 13 -
would finance the construction and improvement of feeder roads and second-
ary roads where justified by the volume of traffic. Government has sub-
mitted a list of roads to be constructed, improved and maintained. The
program would include the upgrading of 90 km of feeder to secondary roads,
the improvement of 120 km of feeder roads, and the construction of 90 km of
new feeder roads. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
Government that it would present to IDA for its approval, on the basis of
annual work plans, a detailed program setting forth timing and supervision of
construction, and design and maintenance standards. The SORAD engineering
unit would construct, improve and maintain feeder roads on force account, as
the Department of Public Works deals only with primary roads. Under the
project SORAD would procure the necessary equipment as detailed in Annex 10.
Secondary roads would be constructed by private contractors and maintained by
SORAD.
4.18      Village Water Supply.  Domestic water supply is a major problem
in the project area, and in particular hinders the expansion of cropping
on otherwise productive land in the savannah zone and the northern part of
the coastal plateau. To contribute to the solution of this problem the
project would provide for the construction of 20 tubewells equipped with
pumps (similar to those already operated by somre local administrative dis-
tricts) and 2 small retention dams to catch the natural runoff, to serve
about 2,000 people each. During negotiations, assurances were obtained
that Government would present to IDA for its approval, on the basis of annual
work plans, an engineering design and construction program of tubewells and
dams for which operation and maintenance expenses would be met under appro-
priate arrangements.
4.19      Storage and Marketing.  To ensure the storage of farm inputs and
output, the project would finance on credit the 2materials required for the
construction of 150 village stores, each 17.5 m in size, by grower asso-
ciations, using the members' labor. To promote the marketing of the increased
crop production and to ensure more favorable prices to growers by increasing
competition among middlemen, the project would build 20 village markets, to
complement facilities which already exist in some villages and have proved
effective. Each market wruld consist of a simple shed to shelter traders'
stalls and covering 382 m , constructed by local contractors. Ten sites have
been selected; the remainder would be selected by PY 3, after evaluation of
relative needs. As is customary the facilities would be administered by the
district authorities.
4.20      Dispensaries.  In Togo, the basic level of health care is provided
through dispensaries each designed to serve 10,000 people or a radius of 7
km. They are staffed with a nurse, an auxiliary midwife, and by one or more
health assistants who make regular visits by bicycle to the surrounding vil-
lages, giving simple care and referring more serious cases to the health cen-
ter. All staff are trained in Lome. Coverage of the southern part of the



- 14 -
region is generally adequate, but falls off sharply toward the north. Thus
the project would, under SORAD's supervision, provide for the construction
and equipment of 15 six-room dispensaries. Assurances were given by Govern-
ment that the locations of the dispensaries would be agreed with IDA, and that
the facilities would be properly staffed, maintained and supplied through the
Ministry of Health.
Studies and Field Trials
4.21      Irrigation Engineering Study and Design  (Annex 11).  Government
wishes to develop irrigation in the Maritime region (para 2.20). The ap-
praisal mission identified a possible development of gravity irrigation on
some 1,000 ha of the lower Zio valley (Map 12155) for single cropping of rice.
The scheme would consist essentially of a dyke for flood protection, a simple
water intake on the Zio, and a canal system for irrigation and drainage dur-
ing the flood season. More information is needed before the scheme can be
appraised. The project would therefore provide funds to carry out the neces-
sary study which would be under the responsibility of SORAD with the coopera-
tion of specialized local agencies. The overall task would comprise a feasi-
bility phase followed by a detailed design phase. SORAD would entrust the
execution of both phases to a competent irrigation engineering consultant who
would be hired for up to 12 months (terms of reference in Annex 11). During
negotiations, Government agreed that it would cause SORAD to engage a consult-
ant before December 31, 1976. Survey would include topography and hydrology
by the MER Rural Engineering Department, soil suitability by the MDR Soil
Survey Services, and land tenure by SORAD. The consultant would prepare a
feasibility report at the end of about six months, and contingent upon satis-
faction of Government and IDA, the second phase would be undertaken comprising
the detailed engineering design, the preparation of bidding documents, and
proposals for agricultural development. To complete this second phase, SORAD
would hire a design engineer as a second consultant for a period of about six
months.  SORAD services would be responsible for the preparation of the
agricultural development aspects, for which the assistance of RMWA would be
made available upon request. During negotiations, assurances were given by
Government that it would cause SORAD to employ consultants whose qualifications,
experience, and terms and conditions of employment would be acceptable to
IDA.
4.22      Field Trials (Annex 7).  The project would finance personnel,
infrastructure and equipment to carry out field trials designed to tackle
two important problems which impede the agricultural development of two
sections of the region: soil depletion in the coastal plateau, and the
Kaincope disease of coconut on the coast.
4.23      SORAD would appoint and equip a research specialist and supporting
field personnel to test promising soil management techniques, especially
minimum tillage and mulching, which have been developed by IITA. The research
specialist would design the details of the trial program outlined in Annex 7
in liaison with IRAT and IITA. This was agreed during negotiations. The
project would provide a budget to cover the travel expenses of IITA personnel
who would visit the project twice a year to give support to the trials program.
The results of the trials would be used in an extension program in the depleted soil



- 15 -
area, hopefully starting by PY 4. During negotiations, Government gave
assurances that it would make arrangements  suitable to IDA, with IRAT
and IITA to carry out the trials.
4.24      The project would carry out a comparative trial of coconut hybrids,
under the responsibility of SORAD's coconut specialist with consultant sup-
port (12 man-months) from IRHO.  Assurances were received from Government
that it would enter into a satisfactory agreement with IRHO including prep-
aration of the details and budget of the trial program before December 31,
1976.
4.25      Project Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 7).  The nature of the
project suggests the advisability of building into it an "early warning"
system to detect problems which may develop and allow either corrective
action or changes in relative emphasis. To this effect the project would
provide funds to establish and operate a monitoring and evaluation unit which
would be charged with determining base line data and organizing sampling as
the project develops. During negotiations, Government indicated that the
monitoring and evaluation unit would be created within the Ministry of Rural
Development or the Ministry of Planning, as a nucleus for a unit operating at
the national level, and agreed to create the unit by November 30, 1976.
Besides preparing quarterly reports on project progress, the unit would also
prepare comprehensive annual reports (Annex 12). The project would supply 30
man-months of consultant support to help design the monitoring program and
assist in preparing guidelines on data collection and analysis. Assurances
were given by Government that it would engage a consultant acceptable to IDA to
prepare a monitoring program and submit this to IDA no later than three months
following his employment.
4.26      Development of Onchocerciasis-Free Area.  The Oti Valley (para
2.06) is included in the ongoing regional program to eradicate onchocerciasis
in the Volta valley system. Consultants financed by UNDP have initiated the
necessary surveys--socio-economic and soil, including aerial photography--
necessary to complete the data bank on which to base a comprehensive devel-
opment plan for the Valley. This work should lead to a definition of the
feasibility studies needed to endow the development plan with specific in-
vestment projects for the Valley. No provision for such studies has been
made as yet by Government or donor agencies. Under the project, therefore,
an allocation of US$300,000 would be made for consultant services to carry
out the feasibility studies during the disbursement period. The studies
would be supervised by the Ministry of Planning. During negotiations,
assurances were given by Government that the consultants employed for these
studies would have qualifications and terms and conditions acceptable to
IDA.



- 16 -
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Project Management
5.01      The project would be carried out by the SORAD Maritime, and by
CNCA for the credit component. SORAD Maritime has overall responsibility
for rural development in the project area, see para 2.12 and Annex 1, but
additionally SOTOCO provides a full range of support services to growers in
the cotton area in the north including extension services. Government has
decided that the role of SOTOCO in the Maritime region should be restricted
to cotton input supply, purchasing seed cotton, and providing specialized
technical support to the SORAD extension service that in future will deal
with all other farmers in the Region. The number of cotton farmers is small,
and only about 1,500 ha are grown currently. A draft agreement defining the
relative responsibilities of SOTOCO and SORAD and procedures, financial and
administrative, for any necessary transfer of staff was discussed at negotia-
tions. Additionally, and in view of Government's intention to reorganize
extension and other farmer support services on a national scale, assurances
were obtained from Government at negotiations that (a) IDA would be given the
opportunity to comment on any proposed changes and that (b) no changes would
be effected in the Maritime region that would adversely affect the management
and execution of the proposed project.
5.02      Existing activities of SORAD Maritime are described in Annex 1.
A major component of its program are attempts to regenerate the depleted
coastal soils; this has come to an end, however, with the conclusion of
FAC financing. Other activities include work with rice, coconuts and vege-
tables, rural road maintenance, and the delivery on credit of relatively small
amounts of inputs. While most of these latter activities would be absorbed
within the course of the project development period by project actions which
would be better planned, financed and directed, there would be some overlap of
'project" and "non project" activities, and possibly a small residuum of "non
project" activities would remain indefinitely. Financing of SORAD under the
project would cover the incremental staff and other costs stemming from
"project" activities. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
Government that: (a) in addition to the incremental staff, it would maintain
the pre-project level of extension services throughout the disbursement.
period; (b) no new "non project" activities would be undertaken within the
project development period without the mutual agreement of Government and IDA;
and (c) Government would cause SORAD to make a comprehensive review of all its
activities including "non project" activities, and by June 30, 1977, would
discuss the findings of this review with IDA. Through these measures, by
the end of the project development period, the overall rural development
program in the Maritime region should be satisfactorily oriented.
5.03      The project would rely largely on the present management and staff
of the SORAD Maritime. The Project Manager would be the current Director of
SORAD, a Togolese (Annex 1). The Project Manager would be assisted by a
Technical Director and an Administrative and Financial Director (Chart 15733
shows the proposed organization and terms of reference for key personnel are



- 17 -
at Annex 13). During negotiations Government gave assurances that it would
employ a Project Manager with powers and responsibilities acceptable to IDA.
5.04      The Technical Director, an experienced agronomist, would be re-
sponsible for field activities and other physical aspects of project ex-
ecution. He would assist the Project Manager with staff selection. He
would be assisted by specialists in agricultural research, training, rice,
coconuts, and vegetables. The training specialist would head the project
training center (para 4.14). The Administrative and Financial Director, a
qualified and experienced administrator, would be responsible for all per-
sonnel, administrative, financial and procurement matters; he would also be
responsible for the Input Supply Unit (para 4.15).
Field Organization
5.05      The project would employ the existing five SORAD sectors, cor-
responding to the administrative districts of the region (Aneho, Lome,
Tagligbo, Tsevie, and Vogan). Each sector is, and would continue to be,
managed by a sector chief who would report to the Technical Director. At
full development each sector chief would supervise 5 to 10 subsector chiefs.
At present there are 23 subsector chiefs supervising 70 field extension
workers under SORAD and another 5 subsector chiefs and 47 field workers
under SOTOCO. The project would deploy an additional 20 subsector chiefs
and 143 field extension workers, giving overall an average of five field
workers for each subsector chief.
Credit Agency
5.06      To handle project credit CNCA would establish the Project Credit
Agency (PCA) in headquarters to be built under the project at Tsevie. A
condition of Board presentation would be satisfactory evidence that estab-
lishment of PCA had been authorized. The terms of reference for the head
of PCA are at Annex 13. He would be administratively responsible to CNCA
but would work in close liaison with the Project Manager and the Technical
Director under similar arrangements to those that are being satisfactorily
implemented under the IDA financed cocoa-coffee project. The head of PCA
would supervise four credit agents who would be given geographic respon-
sibilities depending on actual credit volumes. During negotiations,
Government gave assurances that it would cause CNCA to maintain PCA, and
would appoint four qualified credit agents.
Staffing
5.07      Management Staff.  The Project Manager and the rice, coconut and
vegetable specialists are already in post and are acceptable to IDA. The
posts of Technical Director, Administrative and Financial Director, Train-
ing Specialist, Research Specialist, Chief of the Engineering Unit, remain
to be filled. It is anticipated that the post of Chief of the Engineering
Unit would be filled by a qualified Togolese. International recruitment
is expected to be necessary, however, to fill the other posts. Assurances



- 18 -
were given during negotiations that the qualifications, experience, and terms
of reference and conditions of employment of the personnel listed in this
paragraph as well as the head of PCA would be acceptable to IDA for the
duration of the project development period. The appointment or reconfirmation
of the appointment of the Technical Director, the Administrative and Financial
Director, and of the head of the PCA would be conditions of effectiveness.
5.08      Togolization.  Each internationally recruited staff would be as-
signed a Togolese deputy who would be trained to ultimately take over the
responsibilities of the incumbent. It is expected that the technical direc-
tion would be assured by an expatriate throughout the development period.
The expatriate Administrative and Financial Director would stay in post for
a maximum of four years, the Training and the Research Specialists for a
maximum of three years, and the Vegetable Specialist for two years. The
training responsibilities of the expatriate staff are stressed in the terms
of reference contained in Annex 13. Government gave assurances that it
would promptly appoint qualified nationals as deputies to expatriate staff
members.
VI. COST ESTIMATES AND FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENTS
A. Cost Estimates
6.01      Project cost estimates are based on estimates as of April, 1976.
Togolese staff salaries are based on the current salary scale fixed by Gov-
ernment. Expatriate unit costs average US$58,000 per year and include basic
salaries, allowances, lodging, travel expenses and provisions for annual
leave, consultants supervision, and overheads. Consultant costs average
US$6,000 per man-month, including travel expenses. Physical contingencies
have been applied to equipment and on-farm costs (10%) and to buildings and
infrastructure (15%). Expected price increases total about 26% of base costs
plus physical contingencies. The total expected cost of the project is
US$15.7 million including 11% taxes and import duties (US$13.9 million net
of these) with a foreign exchange component of about US$9.3 million or 59%.
The following table summarizes project costs; further details are at
Annex 14.
