6.03      FAC has agreed in principle to co-finance the project through
a grant of FF 10 million (US$2.2 million). Signature of the grant agree-
ment between Government and FAC would be a condition of credit effective-
ness.
6.04      An IDA credit of US$9.5 million would be extended on standard
terms. The credit would substantially cover the foreign exchange cost or
a total of 68% of project costs net of taxes and duties.
6.05      The proceeds of the IDA credit and FAC grant, together with Gov-
ernment's contribution, would be deployed as follows:
(a)  CFAF 1,940 million (US$8.6 million) to SORAD as a
grant to finance the construction of project build-
ings, roads, water supplies, dispensaries and mar-
kets, to purchase vehicles and equipment, to cover
the incremental costs of personnel and other oper-
ating expenses, and for irrigation studies and
consultant services for coconuts;



- 21-
(b) CFAF 291 million (US$1.3 million) as an additional Govern-
ment equity contribution to CNCA to finance the construction
of a credit agency at Tsevie and to cover the cost of vehicles,
personnel and other operating costs connected with project
credit activities (CFAF 146 million); and to cover the cost
of incremental fertilizers, pesticides and other seasonal
inputs that would be supplied to project participants through
the mechanism of a revolving fund (CFAF145 million); and
(c) CFAF 140 million (US$0.6 million) as a loan to CNCA to finance
medium and long term credit to farmer groups for equipment,
power tillers, rice mills, storage facilities, and coconut
seedlings.
Government would use CFAF 68 million (US$0.3 million) to finance feasi-
bility studies in the areas to be freed of onchocerciasis, and CFAF 95
million (US$0.4 million) to create and operate a Project monitoring and eva-
luation unit. The remainder, CFAF 888 million (US$3.9 million), would be
retained as a contingency reserve. Farmers would contribute CFAF 110 million
to the onfarm costs.
6.06      Except for the costs of rice land preparation (para 4.06) and
irrigation water for vegetable growing (para 4.09), which would be fully
charged to beneficiaries (and which represent a minor portion of project
cost), other costs incurred by SORAD would be for services and investments
which are normally financed out of the general budget and are not directly
recoverable from users. Therefore, channeling project funds to SORAD as a
grant would be justified. During negotiations, Government agreed to establish
at the Treasury a Project account for the pre-financing of SORAD activities
under the Project, and to replenish the account every three months. The
establishment of the Project account with an initial deposit of CFAF 70
million would be a condition of effectiveness.
6.07      As described in Annex 2, CNCA has limited resources of its own
and is forced to finance short-term operations, such as seasonal credits,
with costly long- and medium-term borrowings. In order to reduce the aver-
age cost of CNCA resources, it is proposed that CFAF 291 million of proj-
ect funds (para 6.05(b)) should be passed on to CNCA as equity. The loan
to CNCA (para 6.05(c)) would be made at an interest rate of 5.5% (the Cen-
tral Bank rediscount rate for medium term lending), for a term of 15 years
including seven years grace. The form of a subsidiary loan agreement covering
the above was discussed during negotiations, and signature of such agreement
satisfactory to IDA would be a condition of credit effectiveness.
6.08      As discussed in para 2.16, fertilizers and pesticides benefit from
Government subsidies. Consequently, the project revolving fund, which
would finance purchase of inputs at cost, but, because of the subsidy, only
receive part of the cost in credit repayments, would need topping up by
Government to the extent of the subsidy. This would ensure the input supply
unit (para 4.15) against financial difficultiès stemming from the subsidies.



- 22-
The revolving fund would be reimbursed from the IDA Credit and FAC grant for
the costs of incremental input requirements, repayments from farmers, and
payments by Government to cover the difference between delivered costs and
the price paid by farmers.  Assurances were obtained from Government
that it would (a) cause SORAD to keep separate accounts for the input sup-
ply unit, recording full costs of input procurement and distribution, in
order to clearly identify the full amount of the subsidy; and (b) reimburse
the revolving fund annually on the basis of statements for the cost of the
subsidy. Establishment of the revolving fund with an initial deposit by
Government of CFAF 30 million would be a condition of effectiveness while
IDA disbursements to the fund in the second and subsequent years of the
project would be dependent on Government's having satisfied its obligation
for the preceding year.
C. Credit Arrangements
6.09      Loans to farmers not able to purchase inputs for cash would be in
kind and would be channeled through the farmers associations which would col-
lectively guarantee loans made to their members. Loans would be made on the
recommendation of SORAD's field staff. Credits would bear interest at 8%,
and borrowers would pay a fee of 3% which accrues to CNCA's statutory guaran-
tee fund (1%) and new guarantee funds to be set up for the Project Credit
Agency and the farmer groups (1% each); the latter would be held by the
Credit Agency. This is consistent with the arrangements under the coffee-
cocoa project (Credit 503-TO). Individual requirements for seasonal credit
(fertilizers, pesticides, seeds) would be grouped at the association level,
and the granting of subsequent credit to an association would be dependent on
full repayment of any outstanding credit; repayments would be due at harvest
and a penalty of 3% would be charged for late payments. Medium term credit
for rice mills, storage facilities, power tillers and other shared equipment
would be made directly to the associations with all members as cosigners;
repayments would be made over four years. Because there is a seven year
wait before the trees enter production, coconut credit to cover the cost
of seedlings, fertilizers, pesticides, and hand irrigation equipment (to-
talling CFAF 9,790 for each 1/10 ha with a maximum of 0.5 ha or CFAF 48,950
per farmer) would have a term of 12 years including a grace period of 7 years
during which interest would be capitalized. Such credit would be made to
individuals, with the collective guarantee of all association members.
D. Procurement
6.10      Contracts for the supply of goods other than rock phosphate, which
are estimated to cost US$50,000 or more each, would be awarded on the basis
of international competitive bidding in accordance with IDA guidelines (total
cost about US$3.4 million). Domestically manufactured goods would be allowed



- 23-
a preference of up to 15 percent, or the level of applicable duty, whichever
is less, when comparing domestic bids with those of foreign manufacturers.
Supply contracts valued at less than US$50,000 (the number of which would be
minimized by bulking procurement and which would total about US$200,000) would
be let after competitive bidding in accordance with local procedures, as would
contracts for the construction of 90 km of secondary roads (total cost about
US$1.2 million) and of project buildings, dispensaries and rural markets
(total cost about US$0.9 million) which would be individually small and widely
dispersed. IDA staff have reviewed the local procedures, which are acceptable;
there would be adequate competition. Rock phosphate, costing about US$0.1
million, would be procured at reasonable prices from Government mines. Small
items costing less than US$10,000 would be awarded after solicitation of quo-
tations from at least three suppliers (US$100,000). Inputs purchased directly
by farmers, would be obtained through local shopping (US$0.5 million). SORAD,
which would be adequately equipped under the project, would construct or
improve about 210 km of feeder roads and build two earth dams on force account
for an estimated total cost of US$1.7 million. The project well component
(costing about US$1.8 million spread over five years), would be carried out on
force account by the well brigade of the Ministry of Public Works which is
competent and sufficiently equipped. Consultant services and expatriate staff
(totaling about US$2.6 million) would be obtained according to IDA's normal
procedures. Costs for local personnel and other operating costs, which do not
lend themselves to competitive bidding, amount to about US$3.2 million.
E. Disbursements
6.11      The proceeds of the IDA credit would be disbursed against costs in-
curred in the five year period between signing and mid-1981.  The FAC grant
would also cover five years, but would include start up costs, primarily the
salaries of key personnel (technical director, training and research officers,
and vegetable specialist) beginning earlier in 1976.  The IDA credit and FAC
grant would generally cover all items in the same ratio, 4 IDA: 1 FAC except
for studies and consultant services, and for infrastructure where FAC's
participation would be 15%, and 11% respectively; FAC would not participate in
the financing of the onchocerciasis area studies. The IDA credit would,
therefore, be disbursed to cover:
(a) 80% of foreign expenditures or 60% of total expenditures
for incremental fertilizer, pesticides and imported seeds,
and for equipment for farmer groups (US$0.77 million);
(b) 80% of foreign expenditures or 60% of total expenditures for
building construction; the procurement of equipment, vehicles
and office furnishings; incremental staff salaries and training
grants; and other incremental costs of SORAD Maritime and CNCA
(US$3.28 million);
(c) 60% of costs of infrastructure construction (US$2.25million);



- 24-
(d) 100% of the costs of consultant services for the plan-
ning of development for areas to be freed from oncho-
cerciasis (US$0.3 million);
(e) 80% of the costs of project monitoring and evaluation,
and of other studies and consultant services (US$0.52
million).
The remaining funds would cover a contingency reserve of US$2.38 million.
Disbursements would be against contracts and certified statements of ex-
penditure prepared by the SORAD Maritime or CNCA. Any funds remaining un-
disbursed upon completion of the project would be cancelled. A schedule
of estimated disbursements is at Annex 16, Tables 1 and 2.
F. Accounts and Audit
6.12      During negotiations, assurances were obtained that SORAD and
CNCA would keep separate accounts of their respective project components,
in accordance with consistently maintained and sound accounting practices.
Assurances would also be obtained that Government would:
(a) cause SORAD and CNCA to appoint independent qualified auditors
acceptable to IDA;
(b) submit audited accounts and the auditors' reports thereon to
IDA within six months of the end of each financial year; and
(c) assure that the reports of the auditors would be of such scope
and in such detail as IDA may reasonably have requested.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND FARMERS' AND GOVERNMENT'S BENEFITS
A. Yields and Output
7.01      Yields obtained by farmers participating in the project would
depend on a range of factors including ecological zone, whether a long or
short rainy season, and degree of intercropping. It is expected that the
average participant would take three years to gain full benefit from project
actions concerning arable crops. It is expected further that during this
time the average 1/ yields of all participants would increase as follows:
1/   Non-weighted average of yields in Coastal Plateau and Savannah.

7.02      As new farmers would begin participating in the project in each
of the five project development years, by the end of the project development
period the expected 20,000 participants would be at various stages of yield
development (Annex 4) as well as at various stages of change in cropping
patterns (Annex 5). The estimated total production of these farmers in the
fifth and final year of project development, and the areas of crops culti-

7.03      In project calculations coconut production is expressed in terms
of copra although much production would be sold as fresh nuts. Palms are
expected to start bearing six years after planting and to reach full produc-
tion 13 years after planting, when they will be producing an estimated 1 ton
of copra/ha annually, and to live for an estimated 30 years. At maturity of
the 600 ha of project, coconut plantings, annual production is estimated at
1,100 ton of copra equivalent.
B. Prices and Markets
7.04      In the table below, average farmgate prices now obtained for pro-
duce such as would be produced under the project are shown together with the
estimated economic value of this produce at farmgate, both in 1976 and 1980.
The latter values have been calculated on the basis of either that the pro-
duce could be exported (groundnuts, seed cotton, and copra), or that project
induced production would substitute for imports (maize and rice). As there
is no organized world trade in cassava and yam tubers, or in cowpeas and
African vegetables, current farmgate producer prices are used as a proxy
for the economic value of these commodities. The economic value for groud-
nuts and copra are conservative, as in practice a large proportion of the
production would be consumed internally. In the calculations of economic
values, the forecast world prices of the commodities made by the Bank's
Commodities and Export Projections Division have been used. Prices are
expressed in 1975 terms, and the calculations are shown in Annex 15.

Copra                                35                         48         47
The table shows that present farmgate prices are generally lower than the eco-
nomic value of the product and consequently some upward movement in producer
prices would be justified. However, Government intervenes effectively only
in the pricing of cotton and groundnuts, as a monopoly purchaser for export;
the remainder of the trade in project commodities, apart from a limited par-
ticipation by TOGOGRAIN, is in private hands. Government would appear to be
able to make an upward adjustment in the cotton price, but has little room
for maneuver in the case of groundnuts because the world price is expected
to decline over the next five years. As current producer prices appear to
provide adequate incentives, as shown in Annex 5, and because there is ade-
quate competition in the local market, it is not proposed to make upward
price revisions a condition of the proposed credit.
7.05      Both domestic and established export markets exist for cotton,
groundnuts and copra, and given the small quantities involved marketing
problems are not anticipated. Yams are a preferred staple and the ex-
pected small increment in production should be readily saleable. The
same would be the case for cowpeas which are in heavy demand in both
Togo and neighboring countries. Relatively large annual production in-
crements would be generated in rice, 7,000 tons, and maize 10,000 tons.
Togo now imports about 20,000 tons of cereals (wheat, rice and maize)
and, without the project, this amount would be expected to increase an-
nually. Therefore little problem is seen for absorbing generated grain
production.



- 28-
C. Farmers' Benefits
7.06      About 20,000 farmers are expected to increase their incomes through
participation in the directly productive components of the project. The ex-
tent of income benefits has been estimated on the basis of six models. Three
of the models represent the impact of the project in providing technology
packages, that are designed to increase the yields of existing crops and
permit beneficial changes in the cropping pattern, to typical farmers in
the Coastal Plateau, Depleted Coastal Plateau, and Savannah areas. The
remaining three models cover new activities that would be sponsored under
the project: rice growing, coconut planting, and vegetable farming, and
that would be additional to existing activities. The models, which assume
that the farmer takes three years to assimilate the package and optimize the
benefits, are described in Annex 5 and the results are summarized below:

7.07      Adoption of the technology packages and the new activities will
both require a substantial increase in labor inputs. The technology pack-
ages will require a 15-20% greater farm family labor input, but as shown
in Annex 5 this is feasible since most farm families are underemployed



- 29-
throughout the year. While coconut planting would not require much labor
and the average family could plant and maintain their 0.2 ha without prob-
lems, adoption of rice growing and vegetable farming would create labor
problems. Consequently it is anticipated that participants in the rice
and vegetable components would have to give up some of their traditional
farming activities. The returns per manday employed in these two activi-
ties are sufficiently high to encourage farmers to make such a shift.
7.08      Overall the net incomes from farming of participating farm fam-
ilies in the Coastal Plateau and Savannah would nearly double from about
US$190 to US$350 (per capita, from about US$24 to US$44); while this is a
significant improvement, income levels, even with the inclusion of other
income, would remain at a level well below the national per capita aver-
age of US$250. In the Depleted Coastal Plateau average current production
levels are inadequate to feed the farm family, and it must be sustained by
off-farm employment. Through project actions basic food self-sufficiency
would be attained by this particularly poor group of farmers.
D. Cost Recovery and Impact of the Project
on the Government's Fiscal Position
7.09      Direct cost recovery would apply only to project costs incurred
in irrigation rehabilitation and rice land preparation totalling some US$1.1
million in the project development period. These costs would be recovered
fully through the mechanisms described in paras 4.09 and 4.06.  In the five
year development period Government would expend a further US$13.1 million in
capital and recurring expenditures related to the provision of a basic infra-
structure and moderately intensive farmer support services in the project
area. Recovery of the US$13.1 million would be only through indirect means:
export duties and OPAT and SOTOCO profits generated by project induced cot-
ton and groundnut production, and taxes resulting from the greater economic
activity expected in the project area as a consequence of the increased
spending power of project beneficiaries. This contribution cannot be esti-
mated with much precision. Nevertheless, making the conservative assumption
that project farmers would spend one half of their incremental revenue in
the modern commercial sector, and given that goods traded in that sector
bear an average tax of 20%, the project would at full development generate
indirect taxes of the order of US$0.3 million. This would allow Government
to show a positive cash flow by PY 8 and to recover its contribution to proj-
ect costs by PY 15, assuming the implementation of a policy of reducing the
subsidy on agricultural inputs by 10% per year beginning in 1978 (Annex 17).
VIII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The project's direct benefits would be the increased output of
maize, yam, groundnuts, cowpeas, cotton, rice and vegetables (para 7.01).
The project would also have important non-quantifiable social, institutional
and ecological benefits. Improved health services would benefit about 150,000



- 30-
people, and water supplies about 44,000. At full development, the project
would provide additional seasonal agricultural employment for family and
hired labor totaling about 1.4 million man-days per year. The project
would have a major institution building impact, particularly on SORAD
Maritime and CNCA, and would help create conditions for replicating rural
development projects in the project area and the country at large. The
project would also initiate steps to prevent further soil depletion in the
coastal plateau area, and to reverse soil depletion where it has already
occurred in one of the poorest areas of the country.
8.02      The economic rate of return is estimated at 37% over a project
life of 15 years (30 years for coconuts). Details on the assumptions and
calculations are in Annex 18. The sensitivity of the economic rate of re-
turn has been tested against changes in the most important parameters.
Reducing the life of the project from 15 to 10 years would decrease the
rate of return to about 32%. A 25% decrease in benefits would reduce it
to 21%, while cost increases of 15% would reduce it to 29%. Details of the
sensitivity analysis are also at Annex 18. Separate returns have been cal-
culated for the six production models (savannah, coastal plateau, depleted
coastal plateau, rainfed rice, coconuts, and vegetables), by imputing to
each an appropriate proportion of overhead costs, and of the costs of devel-
oping markets and feeder roads. These rates of return range from 13% for
coconuts to 52% for vegetables. Secondary roads, which are expected to
generate benefits in addition to those estimated for the project, were
separately evaluated and a rate of return in the order of 18% is indicated.
No attempt has been made to quantify the benefits derived for village water
supply and health dispensaries.
Risks
8.03      The project would represent the first major involvement of IDA in
up-land arable crop production in a bi-modal rainfall area of West Africa.
Indeed the project would be the largest externally financed project in such
an area in Western Africa. Throughout most of the region the principal crops
in these areas are tree crops - cocoa, coffee, oil palm and rubber - and agri-
cultural research has concentrated on the requirements of these largely ex-
port tree crops. The food crops of the area have generally been neglected
by researchers and even where technology packages have been developed, the
period and scale of testing at the farmer level has been limited. Therefore
the assumptions concerning yield increments at farm level may be subject to
substantial error and, as a consequence, so may the assumptions regarding
farmer response to innovations promoted under the project. Further, although
in these areas total rainfall and the duration of the rainy season appear sub-
stantially better than in areas further north, where a single peak rainfall
occurs and the rainy season is much shorter, the benefit of such a rainfall
regime may be more illusory than real. This is because the second rainy
season is often too short to guarantee profitable crops; and crops grown in
the area during the wet season often have to be harvested and stored under
very adverse wet and humid conditions. Given these circumstances, the tech-
nology base for the project generally is less firm than for other arable crop



- 31-
projects in Western Africa and although the situation is improving, in partic-
ular, because of the establishment of the International Institute for Tropical
Agriculture at Ibadan, Nigeria, which was specifically created to deal with
the problems of this ecological zone, the risks involved in the project must
be considered substantial. Nonetheless, the critical situation in the proj-
ect area, described in this report, demands that the risk be taken.
IX. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
9.01      During negotiations, the following principal assurances were ob-
tained from Government, that:
(a)  it would consult annually with IDA on prices to be paid
by farmers for fertilizers and pesticides, with a view to
progressively eliminating the subsidy element (para 2.16);
(b)  it would present to IDA for its approval annual work plans,
detailing (a) the construction and improvement program of
feeder and secondary roads, and (b) the engineering design
and construction program of tubewells and dams, for which
operating and maintenance expenses would be met under appro-
priate arrangements (paras 4.17 and 4.18);
(c)  it would consult with IDA on any proposed changes in the
organization of farmer support services, and no changes
would be effected in the Maritime Region that would
adversely affect the management and execution of the
proposed project (para 5.01);
(d)  no new "non project" activities would be undertaken within
the project development period without the mutual agreement
of Government and IDA, and Government would cause SORAD to
make a comprehensive review of all its activities including
"non project" activities and, by June 30, 1977, would discuss
the findings of this review with IDA (para 5.02); and
(e)  it would establish at the Treasury a Project Account for the
purpose of prefinancing SORAD activities under the project,
and replenish the account every three months (para 6.06); and
it would cause SORAD to keep separate accounts for the input
supply unit, recording full costs of input procurement and
distribution, in order to clearly identify the full amount
of the subsidy, and reimburse the revolving fund quarterly
on the basis of statements for the cost of the subsidy
(para 6.08).



- 32-
9.02      Conditions of effectiveness would be:
(a) the appointment, or reconfirmation of the appointment, of
the Technical Director, Administrative and Financial
Director, and the head of PCA (para 5.07);
(b) signature of the grant agreement between Government and
FAC (para 6.03);
(c) establishment of a Project Account for SORAD, with an
initial deposit by Government of CFAF 70 million
(para 6.06);
(d) the signature of an agreement acceptable to IDA between
Government and CNCA (para 6.07); and
(e) establishment of a revolving fund with an initial deposit
by Government of CFAF 30 million (para 6.08).
9.03      Conditions of disbursement would be:
(a) against the purchase of additional power tillers after
PY 2, that the previous tests are judged satisfactory
by SORAD and IDA (para 4.07);
(b) against the purchase of new pumps, that SORAD had initiated
an effective system of collecting water charges (para 4.09);
and
(c)  against the revolving fund in the second and subsequent
years of the project, that Government had satisfied its
obligation for the preceding year (para 6.08).
9.04      With  the above assurances and conditions, the project is suitable
for an IDA credit of US$9.5 million.

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SORAD MARITIME
A.   Current Status
1.        The SORADs (Societes Regionales d'Amenagement et de Development)
were created in December 1965 to be responsible for development, essentially
rural development, in each of the five economic regions of the country.
Objectives
2.        The SORAD Maritime is entrusted with the implementation of the
development plan for its region. Its main objective is to improve the standard
of living of rural populations and the economie and social development of the
region as a whole. Specifically the SORAD is responsible for:
(a) rural education to train leaders and develop farm organi-
zations;
(b) land improvement;
(c) maintenance of collective improvements (dams, canals, planta-
tions, soil conservation works);
(d) development of rural artisanal activities; and
(e) regional organization of cooperatives, contract work, agricul-
tural credit, crop extraction, and marketing.
Management
3.        SORAD Maritime is a para-statal agency with a large measure of
administrative autonomy. Prior to reorganization, there was a three-layer
administrative directorate: a board of directors, chaired by a representative
of the Minister of Planning and including representatives of the Ministries of
Rural Economy, Social Affairs, and Commerce; the Economic and Social Council;
the Chamber of Commerce and Agriculture; the district chiefs of Tsevie,
Tabligbo, Vogan, Aneho and Lome; 10 farmer representatives; and the Government
superintendent; was responsible for approval of the annual program. A permanent
management commission made up of a representative of the region, a representa-
tive of the Ministry of Rural Economy and a representative of the Ministry of
Social Affairs, was responsible for regular over-sight of SORAD activities
between meetings of the board of directors. A director, appointed by govern-
mental decree, executed the decisions of the board of directors.
4.        Following reorganization of the Ministry of Rural Economy in
early 1975, SORAD was placed under the administrative jurisdiction of the



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Ministry of Rural Equipment (MER), despite the fact that many of its activi-
ties remained under the technical responsibility of the Ministry of Rural
Development (MDR). Government envisages turning SORAD into a regional agency
responsible for rural infrastructure under MER, and to create regional exten-
sion services responsible to MDR. SORAD is thus going through a transitional
period, along with other government institutions dealing with agriculture.
Organization
5.        The headguarters organization currently comprises five divisions:
(a) administrative, with. sections dealing with secretariat, documentation,
general services and archives; (b) production, with sections dealing with
coconuts, vegetables, rehabilitation of depleted soils, and rice; (c) train-
ing, with sections concerned with organization, grower associations and co-
operatives; (d) economic and planning, with sections dealing with accounting,
credit, special cassava credit, programs, investment budget, and supply; and
(e) rural engineering, dealing with rural roads, land development and village
water supply.
6.        The field organization comprises five sectors corresponding to
the five administrative districts (circonscriptions). Each sector is admin-
istered by a sector chief appointed by the director of SORAD. Each sector
is divided into subsectors, which in turn are divided into extension zones.
Financial Resources
7.        SORAD resources include an endowment of CFAF 5 million in cash
paid by Government and an endowment of CFAF 1 million for each district,
taken from the account of the Federation of the former Public Corporations
for Rural Action. These endowments are deposited in an account at CNCA.
The SORAD was credited with the goods, properties and cash contributed by
the rural communities following the termination of the Public Corporations
of Rural Action. The SORAD's capital can be increased by self-financing
activities. SORAD can also use funds coming from: borrowings that it is
entitled to make; annual dues of its members; benefits from the sale of
farm inputs; benefits from its marketing, processing or transport activ-
ities; agricultural credit commissions. Until now SORAD has been unable
to achieve financial autonomy, since its field of action is primarily food
crops.
8.        Following is a summary of SORAD's recent balance sheets (as of
December 31):

9.        Financing by foreign agencies.  SORAD has received assistance
from FAC, FED and UNDP through agreements with Government. According to the
statutes, the foreign aid agencies can supervise the financial and technical
aspects of the programs for which they provide financing. SORAD has been
reinforced by personnel financed by FAC, FAO/UNDP and FED. As of December
1975, FAC-financed personnel consisted of an advisor to the Director, a
vegetable specialist and a mechanic.
10.       Financing by OPAT.  OPAT plays an important role in financing
projects dealing with infrastructure (roads, silos). OPAT has also assisted
production activities, such as the financing of coconut extension workers, and
finance marketing operations, although SORAD's role in cotton, marketing has
been assumed by SOTOCO.
Activities
11.       SORAD currently engages in a number of specialized activities in
addition to its activities in the five sectors:
(a) mechanization: this is the "operation regeneration des terres
de barre" (rehabilitation of depleted coastal plateau soils)
based on plowing to incorporate organic matter in the soil;
the operation has been financed by FAC, which terminated its
financing at the end of 1975;



ANNEX 1
Page 4
(b)  vegetables:  SORAD has developed an irrigated vegetable scheme
at Agoueve near Lome, as described in Annex 8;
(c) rural engineering: essentially rural road maintenance and land
development activities;
(d) coconuts: this section is responsible for extension services
to growers, plantation of dwarfs and hybrids,
and operates a non-irrigated seed orchard at Agoueve; and
(e) credit: SORAD has been acting as an intermediary for credit to
farmers from CNCA. A major problem has been that of subsidies:
SORAD has been charged the full cost of inputs, especially ferti-
lizers, while farmers repaid only the subsidized price, the balance
corresponding to a debit for SORAD on CNCA books. The credit
program was terminated with the creation of TOGOGRAINS, the agency
responsible for food grain marketing. With the creation of
TOGOGRAINS, SORAD was stripped of its last remaining marketing
function. However, SORAD has still been providing "production"
credit to cassava growers in order to ensure the supply of raw
material to Government's starch factory at Ganave. The program has
been financed by the factory and amounts to advance purchases rather
than production credit.
B.   SORAD Maritime under the Proposed Project
Organization
12.       The SORAD Maritime would form the management unit of the proposed
project. To carry out its expanded activities SORAD would be reinforced as
described in the following paragraphs. Main features would be the assignment
of a Technical Director, an Administrative and Financial Director, a training
specialist; and the creation of an Input Supply Unit, and a Monitoring Unit.
13.       The Project Manager would be the Director of SORAD Maritime and
would be responsible for all operations. He would assure the coordination of
project activities with those of CNCA, SOTOCO and other agencies in the
project area. He would be assisted by a Technical Director, an Administrative
and Financial Director, and a Chief of the Engineering Unit. A Technical
Director, an experienced agronomist, would be appointed and he would supervise
the work of the sector chiefs. He would also coordinate the trials program
with national and other research agencies. He would be assisted by special-
ists in training, research, rice, coconuts, and vegetables. The training
specialist would supervise the training center to be established at Tsevie.
An Administrative and Financial Director, a specialist in administration and



ANNEX 1
Page 5
finance with management experience, would be assigned to assume responsibil-
ity for all personnel, administrative, financial and procurement matters; he
would also be responsible for the Input Supply Unit. The Chief of the Engi-
neering Unit would be responsible for the construction of roads, wells,
markets, dispensaries, project buildings, whether built on force- account
or under contract. The unit would include a small design section.
14.       Field Organization.  The project would deploy an additional 20
subsector chiefs, and an additional 143 field extension workers, giving an
overall average of five field workers for each subsector chief. The addi-
tional extension workers would be assigned as follows: Aneho: 29 (16 to
coconuts); Lome: 7 (4 to coconuts, 3 to vegetables); Tabligbo: 43 (6 to
rice); Tsevie: 51 (30 to rice, 3 to vegetables); Vogan: 13 (10 to rice).
Input Supply Unit
15.       SORADs has been supplying inputs to farmers on a limited, ad-hoc
basis, as part of its extension function. A problem with imported inputs,
especially fertilizers, has been late delivery The project activities would
result in a substantial increase in demand for seasonal inputs, and the
success of the crop production programs would depend largely on the timely
delivery of inputs. To meet future needs an Input Supply Unit would be
created within SORAD with responsibility for (a) determining input needs
on the basis of estimates collected by the extension services; (b) procur-
ing the supplies; and (c) organizing the distribution to grower associa-
tions.
16.       The Chief of the unit would operate under the responsibility of
the Administrative and Financial Director. He would be assisted by book-
keepers and a chief warehouseman. The unit would maintain detailed accounts
of its operations.
Investments
17.       The expanded organization and activities of SORAD Maritime would
require investments in buildings and equipment. The headqu2rters at Lome
would be expanded by building a second story covering 420 m , on the
pres2nt structure. The training center to be built at Tsevie would cover
80 m . The Engineering Unit would be equipped with road maintenance equip-
ment (Annex 10), and the project would finance the purchase of 35 vehicles
for the management staff, and 48 motorcycles and 202 bicycles for the field
staff (Annex 14).

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
CAISSE NATIONALE DE CREDIT AGRICOLE (CNCA)
A.   General Structure and Performance
1.        Agricultural credit was one of the least successful activities of
the multipurpose development bank, Credit du Togo (CT), which was dissolved
in 1967 and replaced by: (a) a Government agency for agricultural credit,
the Caisse Nationale de Credit Agricole (CNCA); and (b) a semi-public general
development bank, the Banque Togolaise de Developpement (BTD). BTD inherited
the assets and liabilities of Credit du Togo, but CNCA was able to start off
with a clean slate.
2.        Management.  CNCA is responsible to the Ministry of Rural Develop-
ment, and is administered by a board of 12 dir'ectors including the Minister of
Rural Development (Chairman), the Ministers of Finance and Planning or their
delegates, the directors of OPAT,1/ the SORADs,2/ BCEAO, 3/ and BTD, and five
representatives of the farm community. The Director General is appointed by
the Council of Ministers, his deputy by the Minister of Rural Development.
3.        Objectives.  CNCA is empowered to extend short-, medium- and long-
term credits, to individuals or societies actively engaged in agriculture,
livestock, marketing of products, fisheries and rural handicrafts, and more
generally any operations concerned with rural development. Decisions on loan
applications are the responsibility of a Loan Committee, whose members are the
Chairman, two Directors, and the General Manager. A Government Commissioner
attends all meetings.
4.        Organization.  CNCA's head office, which also covers the Maritime
Region, is in Lome. Four other regional branches were opened in 1973 and 1974
at Atakpame, Sokode, Lama Kara, and Dapango in the Plateau, Savanah, la Kara
and Central Regions, respectively. CNCA plans to open local offices in se-
lected areas, particularly at Tabligbo in the Maritime region.
5.        CNCA has a staff of 83, with 27 in the four branches.  The general
service staff (drivers, guards, etc.) accounts for about one-fourth of the
personnel establishment. Since the alternative reorganization, which was
completed early in 1975, stress has been put on the recruiting of personnel
having banking, accounting, or business management certificates. Together
with those of General Manager and Deputy, the key posts in the Credit, Fi-
nance and Accounting, and General Inspection Departments are held by quali-
fied university graduates, and in-service training is now in progress.
1/   Office des Produits Agricoles du Togo.
2/   Societes Regionales d'Amenagement et de Developpement.
3/   Banque Centrale des Etats de l'Afrique de l'Ouest.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
6.        CNCA was reorganized in 1974.  The new organization chart (Chart
15739) includes five departments: (a) credit (loan applications, appraisal
and disbursement); (b) finance and accounts (at centralized level); (c) loan
collection and legal action; (d) general inspection, directly responsible
to the General Manager (control of head office and branches, staff training);
and (e) administration and personnel. A Project Credit Unit (PCU), to admini-
ster loans to farmers under the IDA-financial Cocoa/Coffee Project (503-TO),
was established in June 1975.
7.        Resources.  Over the last four years, CNCA's equity averaged 30% of
total resources; the other 70% was provided through (a) medium-term and sight
deposits; and (b) financial facilities from public agencies, particularly
OPAT. The average cost of medium-term deposits and financial facilities is
high (over 5%), and the sight deposits (mainly from civil servants and compan-
ies) are characterized by rapid fluctuations and many small and costly move-
ments.  Rediscounting facilities are liimited to medium-term credit as long as
the BCEAO considers that local banks, taken as a whole, have enough deposits
to self-finance their short-term operations. Agricultural loans do not fit
with this concept, and CNCA has recently requested BCEAO to reconsider the
matter. If BCEAO agrees, CNCA would have more flexibility in planning its
lending program; but the cost of resources would still reflect BCEAO's redis-
count rate of 5.5%. Considering the Government's rural development policy,
CNCA will require growing resources. Some of these might come from growth of
deposits, through promotional efforts. The larger part would have to be
provided by the Government through increased equity; Government has promised
CFAF 200 million, and CFAF 100 million was paid in 1975 as a first installment.
8.        Lending Activities.  From 1967 to 1974 (September 30), roughly
CFAF 5 billion was lent on a short- and medium-term basis. Some 3% went to
seasonal production, and 9% for on-farm development loans. The bulk went into
marketing credits (73%); the remainder went for non-agricultural loans, 4% to
enterprises (private as well as public or semi-public) and 11% to individuals.
CFAF 840 million went to the five SORAD's for purchase of inputs (CFAF 202
million), equipment (CFAF 66 million), production marketing (CFAF 300 mil-
lion), and for special projects (CFAF 272 million).
9.        Prior to the recent reorganization, CNCA suffered from an inade-
quate credit policy, reflected in superficial appraisal of loans, and lack of
aggressiveness in loans recovery. As a result, as of August 31, 1975, arrears
amounted to CFAF 257 million, 23% of the portfolio. About 40% (CFAF 104
million) of arrears are on non-agricultural loans made to companies. Most of
the balance of CFAF 153 million is on loans to SORADs, mainly on fertilizer
debited to the SORAD at actual cost, under government instruction, whereas the
fertilizer had to be   sold to farmers at a subsidized price.  Measures to
balance the SORAD overdue accounts are under consideration by Government.
Reserves to cover arrears have been provided to the level of CFAF 69 million
(27% of arrears). Heavy losses however are expected for non-agricultural
loans.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
10.       The 1974 change in management, and the subsequent reorientation
toward the agricultural sector, have given CNCA a new lease on life. Non-
agricultural loans have been stopped; loans applications are being more
carefully appraised; and arrears are being followed up (CFAF 55 million
collected in 1975).
11.       Financial Position.  Liquid assets amounting to CFAF 220 million,
or 15% of total assets, and short-term assets of CFAF 837 million cover
more than twice short-term liabilities (CFAF 440 million). Fixed assets
(CFAF 61 million) and long- and medium-term assets (CFAF 299 million, including
net doubtful debts) are fully covered by CNCA's equity. Long-term resources
amount to CFAF 509 million.
12.       As indicated in para 7, it is rather unsound to finance short-term
operations with costly long- and medium-term resources. And it would appear
that as long as sight and saving deposits are relatively scarce in the country,
the best alternative is to substantially increase CNCA's equity.
13.       Financial Results.  Profits were declining prior to 1973, and
losses were incurred in 1974 and probably in 1975 (Table 3). The reasons
are the high cost of resources, fast growing general expenditures caused
by branch openings and personnel recruiting, and the unchanged rate of interest
on loans. A reversal of this trend is being pursued on four fronts: (a)
increasing CNCA's equity to lower the cost of resources, (b) controlling
general expenditures, (c) developing lending operations on a sound basis,
and (d) strengthening loan recovery operations. If necessary, a further step
would be to raise, by one or two points, the present rates of interest (7%
for production loans and 8% for marketing credits).
14.       Auditing.  In addition to the internal controls, CNCA is audited
once a year by the statutory Accounts Commissioner, who is the Director
of Budget in the Ministry of Finance, and CNCA is also subject to the inspection
of the Government Auditor General. As specified in the IDA financed Cocoa-
Coffee project, CNCA is audited by a firm of auditors of international stand-
ards which, with IDA agrement, also audits the project accounts.
B. Consultant Review of Organization and Finances
15.       One of the objectives of the Project Credut Unit (PCU) created under
Credit 503-TO (para 6) was to provide a training ground for CNCA staff that
would be later replicated in the CNCA operations throughout the country.
Provision was made under the credit for consultant services to (a) review the
procedures and structure of CNCA, in order to create the environment in which
the experience of PCU could be used; and (b) make recommendation on the
organization of PCU.
16.       The consultant review was carried out, and its recommendations
submitted, in 1975. With respect to the organization and finances of CNCA,
the main recommendations were:



ANNEX 2
Page 4
(a) CNCA should revert to its original purpose of granting credits
only to the agricultural sector, and all credits to commercial
concerns should be stopped;
(b) all loan applications and decisions thereupon should be made
by a loan committee having meetings at least once a month;
minutes of the meetings should be prepared and signed by all
members of the committee; and
(c) special consideration should be given to the administrative
expenses of CNCA with regard to personnel and further recruit-
ment should be stopped.
17.       CNCA has begun to implement these recommendations, by stopping
non-agricultural loans, and by creating a loan commitee. Regarding the
financial status and personnel expenses, the situation is being monitored
by supervision missions.
C.   Project Credit Agency (PCA)
18.       CNCA would be responsible for providing effective credit services to
the project through a Project Credit Agency (PCA) which would be set up for
that purpose. PCA would make recommendations to CNCA's Loan Committee regard-
ing project loans to farmers on the basis of (a) technical considerations and
advice of the Project Management, and (b) CNCA's financial criteria.
19.       Organization.  PCA's offices would be located in Tsevie, geographic
center of the Project area. All facilities would be located in premises built
by the project. The Director would be a credit specialist with prior exper-
ience in a similar position (see terms of reference, Annex 13). Other staff
would be his deputy, four credit assistants (one for each main Project area)
one accountant, four bookkeepers, one cashier, two secretaries, one driver,
one messenger. The Director, his deputy and the four credit assistants
would be provided with adequate transport facilities. The four credit assis-
tants would each cover one of the four major project areas: West of Tsevie,
around and north of Tsevie, Tabligbo (northeast of Tsevie) and the Vogan -
Aneho area in the eastern part of Maritime Region. The first two assistants
would be based in Tsevie, the third one in Tabligbo, and the fourth one in
Ganave (Aneho District), where SORAD Maritime already has a credit office
(Annex 1, para 11).
20.       Lending Purposes and Terms.  PCA would provide seasonal, medium-
and long-term credits to grower associations of about 7 to 15 members, for
procurement of inputs and equipment, and for land preparation in rainfed rice
areas. No cash credits would be extended. Instead, PCA would directly pay
the supplier for inputs and equipment, and thé Project's Rural Engineering
Service for the land preparation cost. Seasonal credit for inputs and land
preparation would not exceed 10 months, but an extension of two months would
be possible if justified by marketing circumstances. Medium-term credits for
equipment would be repayable in four annual installments. Long-term credit
would be made for inputs and equipment for coconut development, with a term of



ANNEX 2
Page 5
12 years including a grace period of seven years (the gestation period for
coconut plantings) during which interest would be capitalized. All loans
would carry an interest rate of 8% p.a. In addition, a commission of 3%
would be levied on all loans to: (a) cover the statutory guarantee require-
ments of CNCA (1%), and (b) build guarantee funds for the PCA and the grower
associations (1% each). Thus the effective interest would be slightly more
than 11% for medium- and long-term loans and over 13% for most seasonal
credits. These terms and conditions are consistent with arrangements made
under the cocoa/ coffee project (Credit 503-TO).
21.       Loans would be made to individual members of each group, with a
collective guarantee of repayment. PCA's credit assistant would study each
applicant's farming plan, and apply criteria related to financing require-
ments and repayment ability, applicants debts, farm budget, and family liv-
ing needs. After annotating the application with his comments and recommen-
dations, the credit assistant would submit it for a final recommendation
before presentation to the CNCA Loan Committee for decision.
22.       After approval of each credit, PCA would announce the decision
to the grower associations'leader. Documentation would be completed and
signed by both parties. The Input Supply Unit, on the evidence of delivery
orders issued by PCA, would deliver the corresponding inputs and/or equip-
ment to the grower association leader against acknowledgement of a receipt
signed by him. A copy of this receipt would be sent to PCA.
23.       Farming activities would be supervised and guided throughout the
credit period by project extension officers. PCA, through its credit assis-
tants, would be kept informed of each group's credit repayment prospects.
One month before the harvest, PCA would remind borrowers of the date of
their credit repayments. Close cooperation between the project extension
officers and PCA credit assistants would minimize the amount of default.
While repayment would be collectively guaranteed, the main guarantee of
loan recovery would be careful evaluation of credit-worthiness, subsequent
credit supervision, and pressure on borrowers at the time of harvest and
marketing of crops financed. Should one member of the group fail to repay
his loan when due, no loan would be made to the group until (a) the loan in
default had been fully collected in principal and interest; or (b) extension
of the due date had been arranged for the principal, provided interest due
is paid in cash.
24.       Credit Documents and Records.  A credit agreement would specify
conditions and terms of each credit, and would be signed by all farmers in
the association. For medium- and long-term loans, the agreement would
include the loan repayment schedules for principal and interest.
25.       The record system and procedures developed for the cocoa-coffee
project (credit) would be adapted to the proposed project, and no difficul-
ties are expected. Briefly, files would reveal the credit position, in
principal and interest, for each grower association as a whole and for each
member. There would be separate sets of records for seasonal and for medium



ANNEX 2
Page 6
(or long) term loans. Loans applications would be registered chronologi-
cally in number and amount and classified by term and category or purposes;
loans approved would also be registered and classified in like manner. Min-
utes of CNCA Loan Committee meetings would be signed by committee members.
Decisions on loans would be supported by comments.
26.       Financing Project Credit.  A revolving fund established in CNCA
would be used to finance procurement by the Project Input Supply Unit and
would receive reimbursements form IDA, FAC, and Government for the cost of
incremental inputs, repayments from farmers, and Government subsidies to
cover the difference between the actual cost of inputs and the prices
charged by farmers. When repayments are collected from the farmers, CNCA
would reimburse the revolving fund for the principal of the credit retaining
the 8% interest for its services in administering the credit as well as the
3% commission for the various guarantee funds.
27.       In the case of medium- and long-term credits, CNCA would advance
the required amounts from its own resources, for which reimbursement would
then be claimed from Government, FAC and IDA.  Because of the high cost
of establishing the PCA relative the initial volume of credit (in part due
to the artificially low prices of seasonal inputs) and because of the gen-
eral need to increase CNCA's equity, funds to cover investment and operating
costs in the project period would be passed on to CNCA as a grant; funds to
finance medium and long term credit would be on-lent to CNCA at 5.5% for a
term of 15 years including 7 years of grace.
28.       Accounts and Auditing Requirements.  In addition to individual
and group accounts, CNCA would be expected to maintain separate accounts
for medium- and long-term credits, for PCA investment and operating ex-
penses, and for each of the three guarantee funds. PCA would be subject
to continuous CNCA internal inspection and the audit of the independent
auditing firm which, in agreement with Government, FAC and IDA, would audit
project; including project credit accounts.
