TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE PROJECT AREA
Location
1.        The project area comprises with the Maritime region, the southern-
most region of Togo (Mag 11885). It occupies a roughly rectangular block be-
tween latitudes 6 °nd 6  50' North, and longitudes 0° 409 and 1  50' East.
It covers 6,100 km , or nearly 11% of the total area of the country. It
faces the Gulf of Benin along its southern boundary, a stretch of 50 km
between Ghana and Benin, with Lome (population of 233,000) located at the
Western end of coast.
Population
2.        The population of the Maritime region is 806,000 (1l74) and is ex-
pected to reach 1 million by 1980. Population density per km ranges from
25 in the north, to as high as 200 in the depleted coastal sediment area.
Some two-thirds of the population is rural; 50% is less than 14 years of age;
and among the adults the ratio of women to men is 1.35. The rural population
increases at an estimated yearly rate of 2.7%, the urban population at
4.5%.
3.        According to a 1973-74 survey, the rural population is distributed
over 59,600 farms, with each household made up of an average of 7.7 members,
of which 5 are active. There are three major ethnic groups: (a) the Ewe
(40%) mainly west of the Haho river and in the Tsevie district; (b) the
Ouatchi (29%) in the Tabligbo, Aneho and Vogan districts; and (c) the Mina
(15%) in the south of the Aneho and Vogan districts.  While the Ewe and
Ouatchi women participate in agricultural operations, the Mina women are
traders.
4.        Administration.  Each of the five districts of the region (Lome,
Tabligbo, Tsevie, Aneho, Vogan) is headed by an administrator (chef de cir-
conscription) who has administrative and political responsibilities.  He is
assisted by a district council made up of village and "canton" chiefs. The
district administrators are responsible to the central governnent; there is
no administrative structure at the regional level.
Ecological Characteristics (Map 11886)
5.        Climate.  The average annual rainfall increases from 850 mm along
the coast to over 1,200 mm in the Northwest. During the long rainy season
from mid-March to mid-July, the average rainfall ranges from 600 mm to 750 mm;
for the short rainy season, from mid-July to mid-November, the averages range
from 230 to 450 mm.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
6.        Rainfall is the main determinant of agriculture in the project
area. It is estimated that maize and rainfed rice can produce satisfactory
yields when the monthly rainfall levels (R) are more than half the evapotrans-
piration levels (ETP). The following table shows the rainfall probabilities
for Tabligbo and Tsevie, in the productive coastal plateau belt, in terms of
net water budgets (rainfall R vs evapotranspiration ETP) for various lengths
of the rainy season.
Length of rainy season (days)
above 90       60-90      less than 60
----------- % probability -----------
long season   R > ETP          Tab         31          34               35
Tse        51           26               23
ETP > R > ETP/2  Tab          69           31               0
Tse        46           54               O
short season  R > ETP          Tab         10           10              80
Tse          6           9              85
ETP > R > ETP/2  Tab          42           28              30
Tse         35          41               24
While no such data are available for the depleted coastal plateau of the south-
east, the probability of occurrence of rainfall deficits is higher there than
in other parts of the project area. Taking an average long season of 90 days,
it is expected that the yield index for maize and rainfed rice would be af-
fected by the water regime as follows:
water regime       R > ETP       ETP > R > ETP/2        ETP/2 > R
yield index        1.35               1.00                 0.50
On average, the yield index for the short season is 0.80 compared to the index
for the long season. Usually the north enjoys a reliable long season and a
relatively reliable short season; the center has a fairly reliable long season
and an unreliable short season; and the south has a fairly unreliable long
season and a risky short season.
7.        Where rainfall is scarce maximum use should be made of the available
rains. Techniques designed to accomplish this purpose, minimum tillage and
mulching would be tested under the project (Annex 5). The project would also
encourage the adoption of short-cycle, drought-escaping crops such as ground-
nuts and cowpeas for upland rainfed farming. Lowland cropping under natural
flooding or controlled irrigation would be promoted in the valleys.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
8.        Hydrology.  Three main rivers cross the project area.  The Zio to
the west has a flow rangig from a maximum of 80 m /sec during the monsoon
(June-July) to a minimum of 0.248 m /sec in the dr! season (February-March).
The Haho river in the center has a maximum of 50 m /sec and a minimum of zero.
T5e Mono which marks the bouniary with Benin in the east has a maximum of 800
m /sec and a minimum of 0.8 m /second. A continuous groundwater table, with
depth increasing towards the south, ensures a satisfactory domestic water
supply in the coastal plateau, but the groundwater supply in the crystalline
rocks of the savannah is spotty, and the local populations in that zone have
to rely on rainwater storage during the dry season.
9.        Vegetation.  The vegetation consists mostly of a wooded savannah
which is dominated by volunteer stands of oil palm. Stands of oil palm cover
some 10,000 ha along the Mono river, 8,000 ha in the Vogan area, and 12,000 ha
to the north of Tabligbo. The tree vegetation becomes sparse in the depleted
soil area of the Southeast, and becomes thicker to the north which receives
more rain. A relatively dense forest is found occasionally along the main
streams. The depressions and valleys are covered with a grass prairie (prima-
rily Andropogon sp.)
10.       Soils.  The savannah soils overlie the basement complex made up
of acid crystalline formations, primarily migmatite and gneiss. They are
mostly leached ferruginous tropical soils, poor in nitrogen and phosphorus,
with a sandy to sandy clay texture, and depth limited by the underlying rock
or a ferruginous crust. There are local variations: in the West-Northwest
the sandy and hydromorphic character of the soils limit their development
potential; in the East-Northeast (Yoto area) relatively good soils with medium
texture offer good development potential; in the central area (Agbelouve-
Assahoum) productive red sandy clays can be found in spots, and the valley
bottoms are suitable for rainfed rice. The coastal plateau soils (terres de
barre) developed on sedimentary sands and clays are red ferrallitic, deep,
with a sandy to silty texture in the top layer, and a higher clay content at a
depth of 20-40 cm; they are low in nitrogen and potassium, but well supplied
with phosphorus in areas overlying phosphate-rich sediments. Because of their
good structure and depth, they are highly productive, and considered to be
among the most productive of West Africa. The depleted coastal plateau soils
(terres de barre degradees) coincide with high population density (200 per
km2). The heavy pressure on the land has resulted in a drastic reduction or
the abandonment of fallowing, and a depletion of the fertility reserves
brought about by losses of clay, org.anic matter and nutrients through over-
cropping and erosion. To make matters worse, the area of depleted soils
coincides with the area subject to the lowest and most erratic rainfall in the
region (para 6). The vertisols and hydromorphic soils of the Lama depression
are dark, deep soils, poorly drained, with a high clay content, and good
levels of organic matter, nitrogen, and calcium; they developed on marl and
limestone. Because of the expanding type clay, they harden in blocks during
the dry season, and become sticky during the wet season. They have an ex-
cellent productive potential, but two main constraints have to be overcome:
poor drainage and poor tilth. The soils of the alluvial valleys overlying



ANNEX 3
Page 4
alluvial deposits are deep with varying texture, but frequently containing
30-50% clay, which makes them suitable for rice; towards the sea some of these
soils are affected by salinity. The soils of the coastal deposits (sea or
lagoon) have a deep profile, a high sand content with low fertility on marine
deposits, and a high clay content and good fertility on lacustrine deposits;
all have a high water table; the sandy soils are well suited to coconuts;
vegetables are grown on these soils near Lome; the lacustrine soils have a
limited potential because of their salinity.
Farming Systems
11.       Size of farm families.  Traditional agriculture is largely limited
to rainfed crops on upland soils. According to the 1973-74 survey by the
agricultural statistical services, the area cropped annually (including double
cropping) is higher on average in the North (1.7 - 2 ha in Tsevie-Tabligbo)
than in the South wlth a higher population (1.1 - 1.3 ha in Lome, Aneho and
Vogan districts).
12.       Sources of farm income.  Besides field crops, farmers derive extra
income from oil palms (oil and wine) in the South, and coffee in the West and
North. They also derive extra income from animals (cattle, sheeps and goats,
hogs and poultry). In the densely populated areas where the crop production
is marginal for family needs, additional income is secured by employment of
family members in the cities and the trading activities of the women. Farm-
ers in the coastal zone derive a good portion of their income from fishing
in the lagoons.
13.       Cropping Patterns.  Bush fallowing has long disappeared in the
depleted area, and has largely disappeared in recent years in the coastal
plateau belt, except for the northeastern tip, but still extends for 5 to 10
years in the more sparsely populated zones of the North. Throughout the proj-
ect area, maize represents over 80%, and cassava 10% of the cropped area. Of
the areas under mixed cropping maize interplanted with cassava represents 80%.
The traditional cropping patterns would not be significantly changed by the
project, except for the expansion of cotton in the North, of groundnuts and
cowpeas throughout the region, and of lowland rice and some vegetables in the
valleys.
14.       Farm operations are spread over the two rainy seasons.  Soil prepa-
ration is carried out in February-March after the soil has been softened by
the first rains. Depending on the rain, maize seeding occurs between early
March and early May. Cassava interplanting follows two-three weeks after
maize seeding. The heaviest demand on farm labor occurs in July-August, when
the harvest of maize and other crops coincides with soil preparation and
seeding of crops for the short rainy season. The project would stress long-
season over short-season crops to avoid an increased demand in labor during
the peak period, and would promote minimum tillage if its efficiency can be
confirmed under local conditions.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
15.       Land Tenure.  South Togo has a complex land tenure system as a
result of interactions between different traditional system, the adminis-
trative changes effected by the colonial regime, and the effect of popu-
lation pressure in the South. There is a gradual switch from community
to individual ownership, especially in the South. Tenant farming is im-
portant in the overpopulated area of the South (40% of the areas in the
Lome and Aneho districts), but not in the North (Tabligbo district). A
reform of the tenant-owner arrangments is under study by the government;
it would promote agricultural development by giving more security to tenant
farmers. Overall land tenure is not expected to present a constraint to
the project.
ProsDects for Agricultural Development
16.       Filling the food gap.  The region is a net importer of food.  Accord-
ing to a 1973-74 survey by the Ministry of Rural Economy, the Maritime region
has an annual net deficit of some 25,000 tons of maize, 25,000 tons of yam and
1,500 tons of cowpeas. Although these figures are only indicative, they sug-
gest that the demand for the main food crops (except cassava) should provide
an adequate incentive for farmers to increase food production, through a better
utilization of available resources.
17.       Rehabilitation of depleted soils.  It is unlikely that the popula-
tion pressure in the South can be significantly eased through resettlement
(para 18 below). Measures can be taken to ease the situation by promoting
known improved practices. Simultaneously an experimental program is urgently
needed to develop the technology needed to break the vicious circle of soil
depletion and increasing population pressure. Such a program would be car-
ried out under the proposed project. Parallel to this effort, action is
required in the productive coastal plateau area to prevent depletion which
could occur as the result of population pressure and the disappearance of
bush fallowing.
18.       Resettlement.  The sparsely populated areas of the savannah have soil
resources that offer some scope for resettlement. Some spontaneous settlement
is taking place along newly opened tracks. Spontaneous migration to the North
and West is taking place from the overpopulated Aneho-Vogan areas, but in the
savannah area most of the settlers come from the northern regions of Togo.
The major constraints to settlement in the North are: (a) the lack of roads;
(b) the absence of reliable groundwater resources in the savannah, and the
absence of wells in the coastal sediment zone, and (c) the occurrence of
riverblindness in certain areas. Details on the infrastructure situation and
the infrastructure component of the project are at Annex 10.
19.       Valley Development.  The valleys have a good potential for rainfed
rice. they are subject to two monsoon rains with a total aveaging 1,000 mean
yearly. This would be developed by the project (Annex 4). While large
areas of valley soil are suitable for irrigated agriculture, there is a
limited supply of water (para. 8). The total irrigation potential for double
cropping of rice in the Zio valley is about 600 ha with direct pumping without



ANNEX 3
Page 6
storage. The feasibility of a dam has been identified for the Zio, to irri-
gate 7,000 ha. For the Mono river, feasibility studies have been carried out
for a double-purpose dam providing both power and irrigation. The dam would
enable Togo to irrigate some 12,000 ha on its side of-the Mono river valley.
Without the dam the irrigation potential for double cropping is about 300 ha
on the Togo side. The possibility exists for gravity irrigation in the valleys
for single cropping during the flood season. The project would carry out an
engineering study for the development of such a scheme over 1,000 ha in the
lower Zio valley (Details at Annex 11). The proposed project would also
promote small scattered irrigation schemes along the valleys for vegetable
production, using hand irrigation methods.



ANNEX 4
Page 1
TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FARM AND CROP DEVELOPMENT
1.        Technical data on agricultural production in the Maritime region
have been developed through the field experience of the SORAD Maritime, the
work of the research stations, the FAO fertilizer program, and the rehabili-
tation program for depleted soils ("operation regeneration des terres de
barre degradees"). The existing information has already been put to use by
SORAD in programs of technical improvements, but further research would be
needed to further improve production techniques, and research is crucial to
develop effective production techniques in the depleted coastal plateau. This
annex deals with the main crops of the project area, current practices, and
the proposed technological improvements under the project (coconut is treated
separately in Annex 9).
Maize
2.        Background.  Maize occupies more than three quarters of the
cropped area in the region. While it is grown mostly as a single crop in
the North, it is intercropped with cassava in the coastal plateau. Inter-
cropping does not interfere with maize growth, for cassava is planted some
three weeks after maize seeding, and does not compete with maize. Some
intercropping with groundnuts or cowpeas is also practiced.
3.        Traditional Practices.  Traditionally farmers prepare the soil by
weeding with a hand hoe (daba) and burning the weeds; in the depleted area,
however, the practice of burning has largely disappeared. The seeds are
planted in hills with each hill planted with several seeds, up to a dozen.
Because the hills are so widely spaced, and because wishing to minimize the
risks of crop failure, farmers are extremely reluctant to thin out their
stands; the result is that the plants fail to develop optimally because of
competition, while the overall plant population remains well below optimum.
4.        Varieties.  Niaouli 7, a strain developed in Dahomey, has
been replacing the local varieties. A hybrid (Niaouli 7 X ATC) with a
cycle of 100-110 days has been released by the research station and seed
is produced and distributed by SORAD. According to IRAT the hybrid yields
15-30% more than the local populations for Fl, and 15-20% more for F2.
Consequently F2 seed is distributed to farmers. It has been well ac-
cepted by farmers, especially in the South, because of its productivity
and taste. The combination of a modest yield increase and ready accept-
ance by the population makes this hybrid attractive, keeping in mind
that highly productive hybrids have been rarely accepted in West Africa
because of taste. It is susceptible to blight and rust, but there is
no data on the effect of these pests on production. The IRAT trials
suggest that NH2 and a hybrid (NH1 X Mexico 18 with a 116-day cycle)
show better disease resistance and give higher yields. Subject to



ANNEX 4
Page 2
confirmation by ongoing trials by IRAT, their production would be promoted
under the project. In the more humid North, a variety maturing in 120-121
days would need to be developed.
5.        Recommended Improvements.  The package of improved practices al-
ready successfully extended by SORAD would be promoted on a larger scale
under the project. They consist of:
(a)  refraining from burning vegetation in the North when the
land is prepared;
(b) using F2 hybrid seed of ATCx Niaouli;
(c) treating the seed with an insecticide/fungicide mixture
(25% TMD and 25% heptachlor) at the rate of 2 kg per
ton of seed;
(d) seeding early, with an adequate plant population: 40,000
per ha (80 x 30 cm spacing, which requires 20 kg of seed)
in pure stands, 33,000 per ha (100 x 30) intercropped with
cassava or cowpeas, 16,500 (200 x 30) intercropped with
groundnuts;
(e) in the absence of fallow, or fertilized cotton in the rota-
tion fertilizing the long-season crop at the rate of 30-30-30
(kg/ha) in the form of urea, rock phosphate, and potassium
chloride, with N and K sidedressed after successful emergence
of the crop;
(f) two to three careful weedings, beginning two weeks after emer-
gence;
(g) where justified by the level of production, treating the crop
with 2% Bromophos at the rate of 1 kg per ton of crop in the
husk, to avoid storage losses.
6.        In the Savannah, where there are reasonable chances for a favor-
able short season, the improved techniques would be promoted for both sea-
sons. However, there would be no mixed cropping and no fertilization for
the short season. In the depleted coastal plateau, improvements would be
limited to maize grown during the long season which is more favorable; ef-
fective soil management techniques remain to be tested.
7.        Yields.  Current yields with traditional management range from
200 to 800 (kg/ha of shelled grain). Under intercropping with cassava or
cowpeas, yields average 80% of those under pure cropping. Maize inter-
cropped with groundnuts give half the yields achieved under pure cropping.
In research plots, yields reach over 4,000 kg/ha in the North, and 2,000
ha in the depleted area. Yields achieved by farmers under the FAO fer-
tilizer program have reached over 3,000 kg in the North, and 1,500 kg in
the South. Under the project the best farmers can be expected to produce



ANNEX 4
Page 3
2,000 kg per ha in the North. A realistic objective for the average proj-
ect farmer would be 600 to 1,600 kg/ha depending on the area and season
(detailed breakdown in Table 1).
Cassava
8.        Background.  Cassava occupies one-third to two-fifths of the
cropped area of the region, with its importance in the cropping pattern
and the diet increasing from North to South. Cassava is also grown in the
Aneho-Vogan area for the starch plant at Ganave. The crop is usually inter-
cropped with maize and occupies the soil for about one year. The two main
varieties tested by IRAT at the Davie station near Tsevie are Goula (12
months) and Kataouli (18 months). Both have a high starch content; in this
respect Kataouli has a slight edge over Goula, but Goula gives slightly higher
yields of tuber because of the longer cycle. Both have limited resistance to
mosaic and are susceptible to bacterial blight. Growers plant cassava cut-
tings between the rows of maize, mainly during the long season a few weeks
after emergency of this crop. The crop is weeded three or four times, and
harvesting begins 10 months after planting.
9.        Recommended Improvements.  Under the project cassava planting
would be linked to the main rainy season. The research station is testing
a new clone labeled 312-524, which yields 50% more than the traditional var-
ieties, covers the soil for 12-14 months, and has better mosaic resistance.
This clone would be introduced to farmers as the planting material becomes
available. Other high yielding, disease resistant clones are being devel-
oped by IITA in Nigeria. The main improvement promoted by the project would
be an adequate plant population (10,000 per ha). While the FAO fertilizer
program has shown that fertilization was not profitable on cassava, the crop
under the project would benefit from the residual and side effects of fertil-
izer applied to maize and other crops in the rotation.
10.       Yields.  The slight improvements just described would enable proj-
ect farmers to achieve modest yield increases from the present averages of
5-6 ton/ha to 6-8 ton/ha. With the introduction of new clones now under
testing and of new soil management techniques to be tested by the project,
yields could reach an average of 9 tons.
Groundnuts
11.       Background.  While groundnuts play a minor role in the cropping
pattern of the project area (some 1,800 ha), the crop has a good potential
for expansion, because of its adaptation to the local environment, its poten-
tial for soil improvement, and its prospects as a source of cash and high
protein food. Groundnut can be used as a pure crop, or intercropped with
maize. It can be grown during the long season throughout the region, and
during the short season in the northern areas. To avoid failure during the
short season, the crop must be sown as soon as the rain begins, so that it
can be harvested before the soil hardens at the end of the season.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
12.       Varieties.  The local varieties are low yielding, a characteristic
which may account for the lack of popularity of this crop. Variety trials
have shown that the short-cycle (100 days) Spanish-type variety labeled 55-
437 is well adapted to the rainfall regimes of the area. It is nondormant,
and susceptible to rosette (but the incidence of the disease is limited in
the area); because of its good resistance to Aspergillus flavus, the danger
of aflatoxin contamination is minimal. This variety has beeen shown in
research plots to yield 1,500 kg/ha with 300 mm of rain, and 1,000 kg with
200 mm. According to recent results, another variety, 61-24, may have a
slight edge over 55-437 with respect to yields.
13.       Recommended Improvements.  The project would promote the extension
of groundnuts intercropped with maize or as a pure crop. The package of im-
proved practices would include: (a) introducing 55-437, and later 61-24 if
its superiority is confirmed; (b) treating the seed with an insecticide-
fungicide mixture; (c) seeding in rows 40 cm apart, with spacing of 15 cm
in the row to give a plant population of 160,000 per ha (or four rows 40 cm
apart alternating with a row of maize giving 128,000 plants per ha in mixed
cropping); (d) fertilization for the long season with 100 kg of ground rock
phosphate, and under intensive cropping, 30 kg of urea.
14.       Yields.  Present yields of unshelled nuts are 400-650 kg/ha (pure
crop basis). Experience shows that yields levels can be raised by the proj-
ect to 600-1,000 kg/ha (Table 1). For groundnut intercropped with maize,
yields would average 80% of those of pure stands.
Cowpeas
15.       Background.  Cowpeas occupy 2,000-3,000 ha in the Maritime region,
or about 4% of the cultivated area. The crop can grow during the short as
well as the long season (except in the drier South where the short season is
risky); it is traditionally associated with other crops and grown on limited
areas. Cowpeas have a potential similar to that of groundnut in the project
area, for similar reasons: adaptation to drought, soil improvement, high
protein content, assured market and favorable prices. In 1975 SORAD intro-
duced cowpea as a relay crop for rainfed lowland rice: the crop is seeded
immediately following the harvest of rice, without soil preparation, and it
grows on the residual soil moisture. This newly introduced practice looks
highly promising.
16.       Varieties.  The local varieties are of the spreading type; a num-
ber of types are grown, each with well-defined culinary characteristics and
taste. IRAT has released two cultivars of the erect type, which is higher
yielding than the traditional, spreading type: 83-36 with white seed and
a 64-day cycle, and Amouchou 2, with brown seed and a 62-day cycle. IITA
has released two cultivars: Vita-1, a high yielding, early maturing (70
to 90 day), semi-erect, moderately resistant to leafhoppers, and thrips,
highly resistant to anthracnose, cercorpora leaf spots, bacterial pustule,
rust, and several types of mosaic virus; and Vita 2 S, erect to semi-erect,
early maturing, with a high degree of disease resistance. Vita-2 S has
been released for breeding purposes only.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
17.       Recommended Improvements.  The project would promote the expansion
of cowpeas in the cropping pattern (intercropping with maize and relay crop-
ping with lowland rice). Because of lack of firm data, the expansion would
be carried out on a limited scale and progressively. The improved package
would include: (a) the use of improved varieties developed by IRAT and
eventually by IITA; (b) seed treatment with an insecticide-fungicide mixture
(25% TMD and Heptachlor) at the rate of 2 g/kilo; (c) fertilization with 30 kg
of urea and 100 kg of rock phosphate per hectare on depleted soils; (d) a
minimum of two weedings; (e) two applications of insecticide, if needed, for
the control of pod borers (Maruca testularis and Laspyresia ptychora), to be
sprayed in the evening if possible; which is vastly more effective; and (f)
storage of the seed in sealed bags of heavy (1 mil) polyethylene, or in
ordinary plastic bags with fumigation with carbon tetrachloride. The pest
control measures would be introduced progressively among the best growers.
18.       Yields.  There is a lack of data on the performance of cowpeas
under the traditional system, and the effect of improved practices on yield at
the farm level. For this reason, the yields resulting from improved practices
in the intercropping system have been conservatively estimated to increase
from the traditional levels of 250-250 kg/ha shelled beans to 500-600 kg/ha.
It is hoped that this level can also be achieved in the depleted zone with the
expected improvement to be brought about by the techniques developed by the
research program.
Pigeon Peas
19.       Pigeon pea is a minor crop in the project area.  It is grown to
a limited extent in the depleted soils of the Southeast. This crop has an
excellent potential, with advantages such as: (a) a broad range of adapt-
ability, especially to a semi-arid environment; (b) a wide range of uses both
as a perennial and an annual crop; (c) lower incidence of pests and diseases
than with cowpeas; (d) excellent nutritive quality of the fresh and dried
beans; and (e) a genetic potential encompassing a very broad range of genetic
diversity and comparatively high outcrossing. This crop could be valuable in
the depleted soil area because of its good performance under marginal rain-
fall, its positive role in soil conservation and improvement, its high protein
content and its cash value (it sells for about 2/3 the price of cowpeas).
Because of its limited role in the diet, and lack of knowledge of the market
potential, only a modest expansion of this crop is envisaged during the
project life.
20.       Pigeon peas would be introduced on a limited scale in the rotation
in the depleted coastal sediments. They would be intercropped with maize.
Besides using local varieties, the project would introduce to farmers, on
a trial basis, the lines developed by IITA which are relatively short cycle
(100-140 days), semi-dwarf (120-150 cm), and relatively disease resistant.
The crop would not require fertilization or pest control.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
Cotton
21.       Background.  Cotton has been introduced in the North of the project
area, where it is presently grown on some 1,500 ha, especially in the Savannah
zone. Cotton usually follows maize and is grown during the short season, to
ensure dry weather at picking time. The average cotton farmers grows 0.8 ha
of the crop. The Mono type of cotton (Gossypium barbadense) has been grown
traditionally in the North, with yields averaging at best 200 kg/ha of seed
cotton. Starting in 1966, modern cotton production was introduced in the
country in the form of a full package of practices including an improved
variety, improved crop husbandry, and close supervision and support services
(extension, input supply, marketing) provided by a cotton development agency.
The improved system has largely replaced the traditional system. The project
would pursue and expand the program carried out by SOTOCO, the cotton develop-
ment agency in the project area.
22.       Variety.  The present variety, Allen 333 (G. Hirsutum), matures
in 120-130 days, and yields 38-40% lint of 1 to 1-1/16 inch staple. SOTOCO
is presently testing Bou, a new variety with a higher ginning yield (41%).
Bou may give better seed cotton yields, and it has a lower gossypol content.
23.       Recommended Improvements.  The land is earthed up in ridges 80
cm apart. The seed is supplied by SOTOCO after dressing with an insecticide-
fungicide mixture.  It is planted in June-July on top of the ridges, in
hills 25 to 40 cm apart, 3-4 cm deep. Fertilizer (150 kg/ha of 14-21-21
including sulfur and boron) should be sidedressed right after emergence,
according to recommendation, but this fertilizer recommendation is followed
only by a minority of growers. The stand is thinned to two plants per hill,
two to three weeks after emergence, and is weeded at the same time. Two or
three additional weedings are needed.
24.       Cotton is attacked by a number of insects; aphids, jassids,
Sylepta (leaf roller), and Earias during the vegetative stage; boll pests
include Earias, Heliotis (American bollworm), Argyroploce, Diparopsis (red
bollworm) and Dysdercus (stainer). The SOTOCO program calls for six treat-
ments with lindane, or preprothion.
25.       Yields.  In the Maritime region, seed cotton yields average 650
kg/ha. With fertilizer and adequate pest control, yields can reach 800-1,000
kg. It is expected that growers participating in the project would average
900 kg/ha starting with their third year of involvement.
Rainfed Rice
26.       Background.  The valley bottoms of the project area, especially in
the Northwest with more favorable rainfall, offer a flat topography, and
soils with good depth, fine texture and adequate fertility that are favorable
to rainfed rice. The topography is such that soils are flooded during most
of the rainy season, providing near-optimum moisture supply for rice. Contour
bunding is advisable under certain situations. The SORAD Maritime has ini-
tiated a rainfed rice development program which has been readily adopted by



ANNEX 4
Page 7
farmers in the Ave region (around Assahoun). SORAD has been selecting suit-
able idle land, assisting with the formation of grower associations, and
opening up the land by tractor on a reimbursable basis after brushing by
growers. With this modest investment and simple techniques, good results
have been achieved (2-3.7 ton/ha in 1975) and farmers have readily adopted
the new crop.
27.       Varieties.  The variety Ainachen 14, early maturing (90 days) and
coming from mainland China, is presently replacing R66 which was grown suc-
cessfully during the early stages of rainfed rice development and IR 442
which has been used more recently. SORAD is testing the performance of other
varieties: OS 6, IR 5, IR 269-26-3-3, and TOX 7-13-15-7-2.
28.       Land Preparation and Seeding.  After the initial plowing by SORAD's
tractor, subsequent soil preparation was to be done by hand. With 0.5 ha
assigned to each farm family enlisted in the rice program, there is suffi-
cient family labor available for soil preparation by hand during the five
to six week span between the first rains and the optimum time to seed rice.
This method of land preparation, which is labor intensive and makes no
demand on foreign exchange, deserves to be encouraged to the fullest.
Another desirable method of land preparation would be plowing with oxen.
Farmers in the area, however, have no tradition of cattle raising, and
would have no direct access to a trypano-tolerant breed that could be used
for traction; besides, there appears to be a social stigma attached to
cattle raising in certain zones of the project area. SORAD would attempt
to introduce animal traction by introducing a suitable cattle breed, and
training farmers to the use of animal-drawn implements on a pilot scale
in the lower Mono valley, where a field agent has been assigned for this
purpose. This would be closely monitored.
29.       Departing from the initial idea of limiting the use of tractor
to the first year, SORAD has been plowing and seeding rice fields on contract
during the second year, and wishes to continue this practice in subsequent
years by acquiring the needed fleet of tractors. This practice is difficult
to justify because of its cost and its demand on foreign exchange. Moreover,
there is no serious labor constraint at the time of land preparation, and
seeding and soil preparation by hand has already been practiced. Plowing with
a light disc plow costs CFAF 18,000/ha, while land preparation by hand costs
CFAF 2,000-4,000/ha when labor has to be hired. Thus the tractors which would
be operated by SORAD would be used essentially to open up new bottomland to
rice production. No additional tractor would be purchased by the project for
any other purpose.
30.       For those growers who would grow more than the average 0.50 ha of
rice, land preparation may become a bottleneck. The use of power tillers
by grower associations offers a possible solution, since these have been
successfully tested in the irrigated scheme operating with Chinese assistance
in the Zio valley. In spite of costing more than hand labor as shown in the
indicative farm budget, the use of power tillers might be attractive, and it
would be tested by enabling individual growers or grower associations to
purchase power tillers on credit. There are several prerequisites to the



ANNEX 4
Page 8
introduction of this equipment among farmers: (1) the proper type of equip-
ment needs to be selected; (ii) the type of ownership (individual or coopera-
tive) should be tested; (iii) the type of operation (hired operator, contrac-
ting) should be tested; (iv) the logistics of operation in the field should
be worked out; (v) a stock of spare parts should be available; (vi) provision
for maintenance and repair should be made; and (vii) operators and farmers
should be trained in the operation and maintenance of the equipment. Pro-
vision would be made in the credit for the purchase of 10 tillers to be
sold to farmers on an experimental basis. This test would be closely moni-
tored and, if successful, the introduction of power tillers could be carried
out on a large scale, involving up to 1,000 ha (and 100 tillers) by PY 3.
31.       Recommended Practices.  The crop receives an application of 80
kg of ground rock phosphate before land preparation. Urea would be applied
at the rate of 100 kg/ha split in two sidedressings, the first two weeks
after emergence, the second some eight weeks thereafter. The seed is planted
in rows, 30 cm apart, after the first rains have sufficiently soaked the soil.
The rate of seeding is 50 kg/ha (100 kg with Ainachen, a low tillering type).
The seed is previously treated with an insecticide-fungicide mixture (25%
of TMD, 25% heptachlor) at the rate of 20 grams per kilo.  After several
years of cropping, weeds are likely to become a problem. At least two careful
weedings will be necessary. The crop is harvested with a sickle. After
drying the crop is threshed with a a pedal thresher, and winnowed with a hand
winnower. This equipment, together with small rice mills (1 ton of paddy per
day), would be made available on credit to grower associations by the project.
32.       Yields.  In 1975, growers achieved average yields of 3.7 tons/ha
of paddy on 22 ha with Ainachen, and 2.5 ton/ha on 100 ha with IR 442.
Taking into account the large-scale expansion expected for lowland rice, and
the possibility of dry years, it is anticipated that project farmers would
achieve average yields of 2.5 ton/ha by PY 3.
Vegetables
33.       Background.  African vegetables are grown rainfed in homestead
gardens, or intercropped with field crops.  They include Gboma (Solanum
Aethiopicum), tomatoes, peppers, okra, eggplants. Their importance in
rural diets should not be overlooked. Some farmers grow vegetables as a
cash crop on bottomland where the moisture supply is favorable. Farmers
also grow vegetables as a flood recession crop in the flood plains; for
instance vegetables planted in December, after the flood recedes, are ready
for production in February to April, when the supply is limited and the
market prices are high. These traditional types of vegetable production
have not yet received the benefit of extension and supply services that
could help improve production through the introduction of simple packages.
Irrigated, European-type vegetables are grown on coastal sands near Lome for
the affluent city market. Because of the city's expansion, SORAD has dev-
elooed an irrigated vegetable scheme at Agoueve, some 15 km north of Lome,



ANNEX 4
Page 9
with heavy support services. Under this system, project growers manage some
0.2 ha of vegetable crops each with an average cropping intensity of 150%.
The most favorable season is May-November, while the period of December-April
is affected by drought and hot winds (harmattan).
34.       Site selection.  The progressive elimination of vegetable opera-
tions near Lome by urbanization raises the problem of proper site selection
for new vegetable ventures. The Zio valley close to the Lome markets has
extensive areas of suitable alluvial soil, with favorable texture and fer-
tility, not subject to flooding but with the water table close to the surface,
so that they can be irrigated at low cost by hand or by small pumps. Sites
can be found for schemes involving groups of growers. Sites also exist in the
Zio and other valleys for small plots of vegetables that can be cropped by
local farmers to supplement their incomes. Some extension of vegetable growing
on the coastal sands near Lome is also possible, thanks to an abundant water
table close to the surface, but the future of such operations is not certain
because of continuing urbanization and land speculation.
35.       Irrigation.  The irrigatio  needs for this type of vegetable opera-
tion are estimated at about 12,000 m /ha for 10 months (excludin§ the wet
months of May and June), with peak requirements of about 1,400 m /ha in
November and December. The project would promote hand-lifting of water from
hand-dug holes or wells and supply credit for the construction of small
concrete water tanks. As their borrowing capacity improves, growers could
envisage the purchase of small motor pumps. The rehabilitation of pumps for
the Agoueve Scheme is described in Annex 8.
36.       Soil Management and Fertilization.  Current plans for the use of
town refuse, carted to points outside Lome, or the large-scale preparation
of composed cannot be justified on economic grounds. Moreover, the use of
town refuse raises the problem of possible toxidity from heavy metals (lead,
mercury, cadmium, zinc). Properly selected soils have a good structure
and do not require elaborate improvement measures as is the case of the
Agoueve scheme. Proper soil management can be promoted by the inclusion of
legumes such as Crotalaria which can be used as forage, and by mulching as
practiced in the Zio valley with Chinese assistance. Interplanting with
Leucoena glauca could supply nitrogen to the vegetables, as well as support
for climbing crops such as pole beans. Soil fertility would also be main-
tained by liberal applications of NPK fertilizer (130-700-130 per cycle
under intensive management).
37.       Pest Control.  While vegetables such as Gboma are not subject to
insect and disease infectations, others such as tomatoes require heavy treat-
ments. It is estimated that one hectare/cycle of vegetables would require an
average 4 liters of a commercial phosdrin formulation for insect control, and
4 kg of maneb formulation for fungus control.



ANNEX 4
Page 10
38.       Development.  Under the project,  the services of SORAD, currently
restricted to the Agoueve scheme, would be expanded to vegetable growers
throughout the project area. SORAD would promote the use of improved var-
ieties, fertilization and pest control, and organize their supply. In order
to provide adequate technical support, SORAD would establish two technical
support centers, each covering 0.2 ha, one near Lome, the other in the Zio
valley. The centers would be used for variety testing and other field trials
(see Annex 5), as demonstration points and as training centers for extension
workers.
Soil Conservation and Tree Plantings
39.       The high intensity rains that occur in the project are can cause
serious erosion and account in part for the depletion of coastal plateau
soils in the Aneho-Vogan area.  No soil conservation program as such can
be recommended at this stage. The minimum tillage and mulching system that
would be tested in the depleted coastal soil area should provide data with
which to design soil conservation practices in the project area.
40.       Overpopulation in the depleted area and the demand for fuelwood
by the population of Lome have caused large-scale deforestation, which
accentuates the danger of erosion, and the demand on the remaining scarce
fuelwood resources. A full-scale tree-planting program would require careful
preparation and cannot be envisaged at this time. SORAD would however en-
courage tree plantings as part of its extension activities. Three types of
plantings are envisaged. (a) Fruit tree plantings in the farmers' compounds
especially in the depleted coastal plateau: mangoes, citrus, cashew, nuts,
papaya, sapote; seedlings would be purchased by farmers from Togofruit nurs-
eries. (b) Fuelwood for areas where the natural forest has disappeared, and
where the domestic fuel supply has become a problem, especially around Lome
and in the overpopulated Aneho-Vogan area; among the species to be considered
for planting in villages and around fields are: legumes such as black wattle
(Acacia mollissima), neem (Azadrichta indica), Leucoena glauca, Albizzia
lebbek; other adapted trees such as Gmelina arborea (similar to teak), Melia
azadarichta (Japanese lilac), and, in the case of salt affected soils near
the coast, Melaleuca leucadendron. (c) Teak and eucalyptus woodlots would be
promoted by SORAD in cooperation with ODEF on suitable sites; in the depleted
area it is possible that these woodlots could bring returns comparable or
superior to those achieved with field crops; SORAD would therefore encourage
owners of plots of depleted land to convert some fields into woodlots; the
production and economics of these woodlots would be monitored by the monitor-
ing unit in cooperation with ODEF. In all cases seedlings would be purchased
from nurseries of the forest service (ODEF) and distributed to farmers at
cost.
Grower Associations
41.       In recent years, SORAD has been promoting the creation of grower
associations. These groups are made up of an average of 12 members (7 to
15), who usually belong to the same extended family, and live and farm in



ANNEX 4
Page 11
the same area. Initially the field extension workers take the lead in
organizing and managing the associations. For about a year, the groups
function as "pre-associations" (pre-groupements). The groups elect a
leader from among those members who can read and write. When a group has
given evidence of viability, it is incorporated into a full-fledged grower
association, which has legal status. The members contribute CFAF 1,000
each to the capital of the group.
42.       When several active groups function in a given village, they
form a village committee, which is made up of 4 to 5 members who elect
their president. Village committees represent the nuclei of future coop-
eratives.
43.       By the end of 1974, 179 full fledged groups were active in the
region, encompassing 2,218 farmers and with an aggregate capital of CFAF 2.3
million. The movement has been most successful inthe Tabligbo district,
where 83 stable groups are operational, and 36 pre-groups have been formed.
In that district some 50% of the associations' capital was formed from the
sale of crops grown on collective fields managed by the members. This data
shows that a sound basis exists for the expansion of grower association in
the project area.

FARM MODELS AND BUDGETS
1.        Indicative farm models developed for the main ecological zones of
the project area and for specialized operations (rainfed rice, vegetables,
coconuts) are described in Tables 1 to 9. It should be kept in mind that the
data on which the models are built is limited, and the information presented
in the tables represents only rough averages. The farm plans in Tables 1, 3
and 5 include intercropping patterns. For intercropping situations, the
tables indicate the net area devoted to each crop, together with gross area
intercropped (in parenthesis). The net areas are used in the computation of
inputs and output. The gross intercropped area should be counted only once
in the computation of the total farm area. In the case of cassava, the same
value has been ascribed to gross and net areas, because this crop is always
grown in association with maize; yields would not be increased in pure
cropping, as cassava is not affected by association.
2.        Farm Budget Indicators.  From these farm budgets, to assess farmer
incentives to adopting improved technology, and the attending risks, several
indicators have been calculated. The investment incentive, defined as the
net cash income at full development as a multiple of the net cash income be-
fore development, is an index of the economic return to a subproject at the
farm level. The income incentive, defined as the incremental cash income as
a multiple incremental production costs, represents the yearly incremental
value/cost ratio and is an index of the incentive for farmers to pursue
improvements. In West Africa, it is considered that, because of the risk
factor, this ratio should be in the vicinity of two to provide adequate
incentives. The risk index, defined as production cost as a multiple gross
income (net of family consumption), is an index of the likelihood that far-
mers will secure a reasonable return from their outlays for purchased in-
puts. Because of liquidity problens, farmers are reluctant to risk debt
levels that are out of proportion with potential incomes. The risk factor
therefore represents a meaningful measure of farmers' readiness to adopt
improved practices that result in significant debt servicing.
3.        On the depleted coastal plateau (Table 4), the farm budget
indicates a negative family income under the traditional system. This
negative figure is confirmed by the fact that farm families derive addi-
tional income from small animals, oil palm, and the trading activities of
the women. The low level of production makes it relatively costly and
risky to adopt improved techniques. It is assumed that by PY 4 new tech-
nology will have been developed that would enable farmers to improve pro-
duction and incomes with low levels of additional inputs. The high risk
indicated for PY 2 and PY 3 will in fact be reduced because of the high
fertilizer subsidy which is not indicated in the budget.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        In the coastal plateau and savannah areas, the potential is high
for a positive impact of improved practices, and fertilizer subsidies would
reduce risks below the levels indicated in Tables 2 and 6.
5.        The budgets for the rice growers (Table 7) indicate favorable in-
comes and adequate incentives. The comparison between soil preparation by
hand and by power tiller for PY 4 (full development) shows a significantly
higher risk and lower net income from the use of power tillers. Yet the
level of income might be sufficient to incite rice growers to mechanize
soil preparation. The budget does not take into account the upland crops
that may be grown by the farmers, at least initially. Farm labor has been
included in the production cost for rice farms.
6.        The vegetable budgets (Table 8) also includes labor costs in the
production costs. It shows that high cash incomes can be achieved on 0.1
ha, and that growers are fully capable of repaying water charges including
the depreciation of investments in irrigation facilities.
7.        The coconut budgets (Table 9) indicate the logn-term profitability
of renovating the groves. Coconut growers, however, have to rely on medium-
term credit and on a grace period for repayments.

TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
FIELD TRIALS, SEED MULTIPLICATION, PROJECT MONITORING AND EVALUATION
A. Field Trials
Research Priorities in the Project Area
1.        Agronomic research in the project area is carried out primarily
by the French Institute for Food Crop Research (IRAT), which operates a
station at Davie near Tsevie, and conducts trials in the depleted zone.
To build up its research capability the Togolese Government has opened a
research center (Institut Polyvalent) at Cacavelli, near the capital, and
has a core of researchers undergoing training abroad. The IRAT program has
been focusing on the development of maize hybrids, the development or adop-
tion of improved groundnut and cowpea varieties, and the determination of
fertilizer requirements. The program has made it possible to extend to
farmers packages of improved practices for growing maize, groundnuts,
cassava, cowpeas (Annex 4). A package has also been developed by the
fiber-crop research institute (IRCT) for cotton.
2.        IRAT is presently working on the development of a higher yield-
ing, blight- and rust-resistant hybrid maize; disease-resistant cassava
clones; and optimum intercropping patterns. These programs would benefit
from close liaison with the work of the International Institute of Tropical
Agriculture (IITA - Ibadan, Nigeria). Under the project such coordination
would be encouraged as a by-product of the field trials on the utilization
of depleted soils in the coastal plateau.
3.        A major unresolved problem is that of the management of the
depleted coastal plateau soils. Experience shows that the package devel-
oped to date by IRAT does not allow farmers to significantly improve their
incomes. The reason is that no practical way has yet been found to restore
the productive capacity of these soils. The rehabilitation program ("opera-
tion regeneration des terres de barres") carried out by SORAD over the last
five years, consisting essentially of mechanical land preparation for timely
seeding and the incorporation of green manure, has failed to develop an eco-
nomic, long-term formula to that end. The vicious circle resulting from
the heavy population pressure and the progressive depletion of the soils
requires that the problem of soil rehabilitation in the depleted area be
given high priority in any development program for the Maritime Region.
Soil Management Trials - Depleted Coastal Plain Soils
4.        The available evidence suggests that the depleted soils of the
coastal sediment zone have lost much of their nitrogen, potassium and or-
ganic matter reserves through intensive cropping without fallow or fertil-
ization. The soils have also been subject to erosion and leaching resulting



ANNEX 7
Page 2
in loss of the clay fraction. "Operation regeneration", which constituted
the core of the program carried out thus far by SORAD on the depleted soils
of the coastal plateau, based on plowing in crop residues, has probably
accentuated erosion and leaching.
5.        Recent work at IITA has shown the benefits of minimum tillage
and mulching in the management of humid tropical soils. These practices
have been found to improve the soil moisture regime and the organic matter
level, minimize erosion, reduce soil temperature (high temperatures may
depress the germination of maize), and eventually minimize the weed prob-
lems. While yield levels may not be increased initially, there is evidence
that these practices, as part of an improved package, will improve soil pro-
ductivity and farm income in the long run. At the same time they signifi-
cantly reduce labor requirements, for labor is a major bottleneck at soil
preparation time, and they can be adopted with minor investment (essentially
herbicide). A field trial program designed to test these techniques on the
depleted coastal sediment soils is urgently needed and would be organized
under the Project.
6.        The program would involve the following field tests:
(a) Comparison of: minimum tillage (with the use of herbicide) and
mulching; traditional hand tillage; and the incorporation of crop
residues by plowing. Each trial would be under the responsibility
of a field assistant; and would be replicated on depleted, moderately
depleted, and non-depleted soils. Similar trials are to be carried
out in other tropical countries of West Africa and other continents
as part of the international network of soil and water conservation
research in the tropics, spearheaded by IITA. The program can
therefore benefit from regional and international linkages.
(b) Cover crops including pueraria, pigeon peas, stylosanthes;
(c) Sorghum ratooning, which has shown promise; and
(d) Comparison of dates and depths of planting of maize (with a
view to securing maximum utilization of the available moisture).
The trial would be directed by the projects' research specialist.
7.        Regional Cooperation.  The Togolese research program has ben-
efited from a certain degree of regional cooperation promoted by IRAT
and IRCT, which operate in a number of tropical countries. A recent
development in the field of research in tropical agriculture has been
the creation of a network of internatinal institutes. The level of
financing, the availability of sophisticated laboratory equipment,
and staffing with scientists of top caliber, enable thse instutes to
carry out fundamental research which a country of the size of Togo can-
not afford to pursue individually. Togolese research, however, could
derive substantial benefits from obtaining scientific support for its



ANNEX 7
Page 3
activities from international institutes, especially IITA which is rela-
tively close. Togolese research could foeus on adaptive work, building
on basic data developed by IITA, and deriving full benefits from regional
cooperation. Togolese research can also be strengthened by the technical
support and training facilities offered by IITA. The program outlined in
para 6 was designed with the help of IITA, which would participate in the
preparation of the detailed trial plans, would make periodic visits to
to the project area for consultations, and would provide support services
(analytical, statistical> as needed. It is anticipated that in carrying
out this program IITA scientists would visit the project area twice a year.
8.        Training.  While developing the information needed to rehabilitate
the depleted coastal plateau soils, the research component of the project
would also strengthen the Togolese agricultural research effort through
training Togolese staff.  Provision would be made for financing training
Togolese staff at IITA and other institutions. IITA is equipped to carry
out training programs in production research on maize, rice and cassava,
the main crops grown in the project. Training sessions are conducted for
periods of some six months, enough to cover a cropping cycle. It is anti-
cipated that six Togolese engaged in the experimental program would undergo
each a six-month training session at IITA.
Coconut Trials
9.        The coconut research program is described in Annex 9. The work
would be carried out by the coconut specialist, with support by a coconut
consultant, supplied by IRHO, and assistance by the research specialist for
the design and implantation of the trial.
Vegetable Trials
10.       SORAD would establish two 0.2 ha technical support centers (Annex
4) for vegetables, one near Lome, the other in the Zio valley. The centers
would have for their main objective the comparative testing of improved
varieties brought in from various parts of the world. Another objective
would be the testing of soil management techniques designed to reduce the
reliance on town refuse, which can be costly and poses the danger of heavy
metal contamination. The trial program would therefore include tests on
mulching and the introduction of legumes for soil improvement. The vege-
table trial program would be under the responsibility of a vegetable spe-
cialist who is already assigned to SORAD with support from the research
specialist (para 6) on the soil management aspects. The vegetable trials
program would be prepared within three months of credit effectiveness and
submitted to the Association for comments.
B. Seed Multiplication
11.       The success of the technological packages to be extended to
farmers by the project depends in good part on the use of quality seed
of improved varieties of rice, groundnut, cowpea, and hybrid maize.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
12.       Seed production calls for careful quality control and enforcement
of standards and a high level of technical knowhow. The present facilities
and staffing are inadequate to meet the expanding seed requirements of the
project, shown in Table 1. The project would therefore reinforce the seed
production program for the Maritime region. The program could serve as
model on which a national seed multiplication service could be built.
13.       SORAD presently operates two seed multiplication centers:  Dagbati
covering 16 ha and devoted primarily to hybrid maize seed production; Agbatope
covering 2 ha, essentially for rice seed production.  Production and quality
control are handled by extension workers but there is no machinery for the
enforcement of quality standards. SORAD has also been purchasing rice seed
from farmers, by offering a premium of CFAF 5 per kilo over the market price.
The IRAT station at Davie produces breeder seed of maize and cowpeas, and has
been increasing the seed stock of cowpeas (bred by the station) and groundnuts
(imported) on a limited scale. In the case of cotton, the seed supply is
assured by IRCT through SOTOCO. The seed production aspects for coconuts
are covered in Annex 9.
14.       The Dagbati farm has been producing FI and F2 maize seed, with a
total capacity of some 32 ton. Because of the increasing demand for hybrid
seed, Dagbati will have to focus on the production of FI from parents sup-
plied by IRAT, while the production of F2 seed will have to be contracted out
to progressive farmers who would receive a premium of CFAF 5 per kilo over
the market price. SORAD estimates the cost of collecting, treating, storing
and distributing the seed at CFAF 10 per kilo. At full development the F2
seed requirement would be close to 400 ton, equivalent to 2 ton of Fl.
15.       The Dagbati farm would also produce registered seed of groundnuts
and cowpeas from foundation seed supplied by IRAT. At full development the
requirements for registered groundnut seed produced under contract by prog-
ressive farmers would be 170 ton, corresponding to some 17 ton of registered
seed to -be produced by the Dagbati farm. This production will tie up over
10 ha out of the available 16 ha. SORAD should therefore consider acquiring
additional land, which presents no obstacle. The seed multiplication farm
would also be used in the field trial program. The project would provide
for the construction of a store, and would provide equipment (Table 2) for
the Dagbati farm. The present part-time seed production officer would be
appointed full time to manage the farm and administer the contracts with
seed outgrowers. Arrangements would be made for his training on seed pro-
duction at IITA, which is prepared and equipped to do so. The requirements
for certified rice seed would reach 100 ton per year at full development,
requiring some 4 ton of registered seed to be produced by SORAD at Agbatope,
under the supervision of the seed production officer.
16.       In line with its ongoing arrangements with SORAD, the IRAT
station at Davie would continue to produce breeder and foundation seed, and
would be responsible for quality control of the seed produced by outgrowers
through field inspections and seed testing.  To conduct this expanded program
IRAT has requested the appointment of a seed specialist; such specialist



ANNEX 7
Page 5
would be financed by the project and would undergo training at IITA. The
project would build and equip a seed testing laboratory at Davie (Table 2).
C. Project Monitoring and Evaluation
17.       At the farm level, the monitoring and evaluation unit would
be responsible for collecting the following basic data:
-    total population; number of actives, distributions by age and sex;
-    farm inputs, equipment;
-    pattern of land-use (crops, yields, production);
-    use of credit; and
-    marketing details.
Most of the data would be available from the questionnaires of the credit
applications, and from information supplied by the extension services.
A major function of the monitoring unit would be to analyze the data provided
by other services, and collect additional information when the data provided
by other services is insufficient. Another major function of the monitoring
unit would be to investigate farmers' opinions vis-a-vis the innovations
promoted by the project. It would concentrate in areas where important
innovations are to be introduced (depleted coastal plateau, coconuts).
18.       At the project level, using data from farm survey, monitoring and
evaluation, procedures would be designed for the statistical analysis of:
-    annual production and revenues, both for project participants
and outsiders, plus a comparison with appraisal estimates;
-    actual costs, compared with those appraised;
-    farmers' response to the project's technical packages, i.e.
land improvement, cropping patterns and use of inputs;
-    farmers' response to the project and grower associations;
-    response to new programs:  rice, vegetables, coconuts;
-    effectiveness of the credit scheme;
-    technical, economic and social constraints; and
-    impact of the project on income distribution.

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
IRRIGATION
AGOUEVE VEGETABLE REHABILITATION
Proposed Rehabilitation and Justification
1.        The Agoueve Vegetable scheme, covering 20 ha with 80 growers, is a
high cost investment because of: (a) choice of marginal soil (depleted
coastal plateau soil); (b) an intricate capital-intensive irrigation network
including hose sprinkling; and (c) uneconomic upgrading of the soil through
the use of town refuse, which is carted from Lome over 15 km. Other problems
have been (a) a high absentee rate of growers, most of whom live in Lome;
(b) SORAD's failure to recover the depreciation and operating costs through
water changes; and (c) a marginal water supply from the branch of the Zio
that feeds the pumps (see para 8). In spite of these shortcomings the scheme
has been a technical success, with production reaching adequate and profit-
able levels.
2.        The scheme is served by two four-year-old diesel pumps which have
a low operating efficiency. The.pumps have deteriorated rapidly due mainly
to lack of spare parts (the pumps were purchased as discontinued models).
After only four years of operation, the efficiency is estimated to have
decreased from 70% at the time of purchase to some 30% at present. As a
result, a gradual shortage of irrigation water is occurring, and the situa-
tion would worsen progressively in the absence of rehabilitation. The current
operating cost of the diesel pumps is estimated at about CFAF 120,000/ha
(US$530/ha) nearly half of which is direct--fuel and labor--and the rest capi-
tal recovery costs (Table 1).
3.        Failure to replace the pumps would result in the progressive aban-
donment of vegetable production presently worth some US$20,000 a year, and a
severe loss of income and employment for the 80 participating growers. Be-
cause of sunk costs, the rehabilitation program is economically sound, as it
would be limited to the replacement of pumping facilities, namely:
(1)  replacing the two existing pumps with new electrical
driven pumpî each of 50 HP capable of discharging
about 140 m /hour under the dynamic hydraulic pressure
of about 60 m. One of these new pumps would serve as
stand-by; and
(ii) improving the existing pump house and intake.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
Water Requirements and Pump Capacity
4.        Since little is known about the scheme's use of water drawn by the
existing diesel pumps, the irrigation water requirements are estimated based
on the Blaney and Criddle formula (Table 2). The estimate takes into account
the following field data:
(i) average crop coefficients (kc) for year-round cultivation;
(ii) effective rainfall expectation at about 70% of the observed
average monthly rainfall;
(iii) overall irrigation efficiency at 70% (water delivered
through the existing pipe system); and
(iv) 80% of the scheme area cropped.
5.        On these assumptions, an average of about 240,000 m3 of water needs
to be pumped annually to serve the 20 ha of vegetables. The peak demand is
about 30,000 m3 per month in November, December and March.  On the basis of
eight hours per day pumping for 26 days in the peak months, the pumps would have
to be capable of discharging about 140 m 3/hr.  To lift this water and deliver
it through the existing pipe system, the pump would have to operate against a
hydraulic pressure of about 60 m.  Under these conditions, the proposed two
new pumps of 50 HP each would be adequate - one to be reserved for stand-by
(Table 3).
6.        The pumping station is fed year-round by back-up water overflowing
from the Zio River system in its lower flood plain.  The water is adequate
except in drought years (once in the last four years) for a period of about
a month during February - March when the water withdraws to a level too
low for pump operation. An allowance for this has been included in the
rate of return analysis (Annex 18).
Electrical Versus Diesel Pumps
7.        As shown in Tables 4 and 5, the installation of electric pumps
would result in higher investment costs over diesel (CFAF 10.5 million,
including 5 million for power connection, as against 8.6 million for diesel),
but it would result in a cost saving for operation and maintenance of about
CFAF 600,000 yearly (about CFAF 2.4 million as against CFAF 3.0 million with
diesel), taking into account the repayment of the power line at 8% interest
for a period of 30 years. Electric motors are also easier to operate and
maintain than diesels. Thus, the electric alternative is clearly superior--
both economically and technically--to the diesel. A power connection would be
required for the installation of an electric pumping system. The connection
would be made by CEET (Compagnie Energie Electrique du Togo) to be charged to
the project, according to discussions with CEET at appraisal.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
Implementation and Cost Estimates
8.        The Engineering Unit of SORAD would be responsible for the pre-
paration of the final and detailed specifications, purchase and installa-
tion of the new pumps. The principal specifications of the new pumps are
given in Table 8.
9.        SORAD would organize the power connection for the pumping station
through CEET. The power connection work, however, is not included in the
project; it would be a complemental task that SORAD and CEET would jointly
undertake. The charge to SORAD (CFAF 5 million) has been included in the
economic analysis.
10.       The total project rehabilitation cost would be about CFAF 5.5
million (US$24,400) including 15% physical contingencies. The annual oper-
ating cost of the pumping station is estimated at CFAF 2.4 million or about
CFAF 120,000/ha (US$530/ha), 60% of which is the direct cost and the re-
mainder capital recovery of the pumps and connected power line based on 8%
interest (Table 4). This is about what SORAD is now paying (para 2).

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
RENOVATION OF COCONUT PLANTINGS
Constraints to Coconut Production
1.        Coconuts in Togo chiefly cover the coastal sands, 1 to 4 km wide,
that stretch East-West over 50 km between the sea shore and the lagoons.
Coconut groves are also scattered throughout the coastal plateau, especially
on sandy inland valleys. While coconut plantings make the best use of the
coastal sands, the economic production of this crop suffers from three major
constraints: marginal rainfall, the Kaincope (Cape St. Paul Wilt) disease,
and poor management.
2.        Rainfall.  Rainfall in the coastal belt averages 750-800 mm in
a bimodal pattern, while coconuts need a minimum of 1,500 mm of well-distri-
buted rainfall for optimal production. In neighboring countries with favor-
able rainfall (West Ghana, Ivory Coast), traditionally maintained coconut
groves of the West African Tall variety yield an average of 700-800 kg/ha
of copra per year. In Togo, average production of healthy plantings is about
500 kg/ha. For this reason Togo probably cannot hope to compete on the world
market for coconut products. The performance of hybrid coconuts under Togo
conditions has yet to be tested on a commercial scale. Smallholder production
for the local market, however, clearly is justified, particularly in the case
of coastal sands where there is virtually no alternative cash crop.
3.        Kaincope Disease.  The disease appeared in Togo in 1932.  The
causal agent has not been identified but seems to be associated with the
presence of mycoplasms and to be related to lethal yellowing disease in
Jamaica and Florida and the Kribi disease in Cameroon. The diseased trees
are characterized by a dropping of the nuts, and a progressive yellowing
followed by drying out of the foliage, starting with the external leaves.
The trees eventually die and the disease spreads to neighboring trees in
"oil-slick" fashion. Out of some 5,000 ha, the disease has wiped out over
4,000 ha. Copra exports which averaged 4,000 to 5,000 per year and reached
a peak of 7,400 ton in 1957 have now ceased.
Disease Resistance
4.        The only known way to cope with the Kaincope disease is to plant
disease-resistant varieties. The diseased trees in Togo are of the West
African Tall type which exhibits no resistance. Dwarf types have been shown
in Jamaica to be resistant to lethal yellowing, a similarly devastating
disease although not identical to Kaincope. For this reason a total of 8,500
dwarf palms were planted from 1962 to 1967 on about 50 ha. About 8 percent
of the palms are natural hybrids between Tall and Drarf. Observations made by
IRHO in 1974 on twelve-year old palms showed that, out of 8,500 trees only two
had died, possibly but not definitely from Kaincope.  Dwarfs and hybrids thus



ANNEX 9
Page 2
appear to have a reasonable degree of resistance to the disease. Impor-
tantly the natural hybrids have additional valuable qualities including (a)
a measure of drought resistance; (b) early bearing, starting to yield 5-7
years after planting; (c) high yielding, 2 ton/ha of copra per year; (d)
copra unlike the dwarfs which produce copra with some undesirable quality
features.
6.        The performance of the hybrids in Togo, while not sufficient to
justify a large-scale industrial type program of replanting, does justify
a limited, low-risk program of renovation of smallholder plantations. Such
a program would be carried out under the project and be complemented by a
comparative field trials of hybrids.
Proposed Renovation Program
7.        The project would involve the progressive renovation of 600 ha
of coconut groves and would involve 2,000 smallholders, each replanting a,
total of 1/10 ha (15 trees) per years, up to a maximum of 1/2 ha over 5
years. The program would require the deployment of 20 extension workers
(1 per 100 growers) to supervise planting and management of the groves,
and to ensure the supply of planting material and inputs.
8.        Technical package.  Seedlings, produced by the project, would be
supplied in polyethylene bags and would be planted on the triangle, 8.5 m.
apart (160 trees per ha) at the beginning of the main rainy season. The
size of planting holes would be 1 x 1 x 1 m. and several layers of husks
would be placed at the bottom of the hole, together with 100 grams each
of ground rock phosphate, urea and kieserite, and 200 grams of potassium
chloride. The young plantation would be planted with pueraria as a cover
crop at the rate of 15 kg/ha of seed. The young trees would be watered as
necessary in the first two dry seasons. The following fertilizer program
would be carried out after planting:

9.        Seed Production.  For the first year of the program, the project
would have to rely on lmports for the supply of germinated hybrid seed nuts.
The most likely source is the seed garden of Seme-Podji in Benin. Meanwhile,
the Project would select 4 ha of dwarf mother trees among the groves planted
on the coastal sands (consultants have determined that the grove established
and managed by SORAD at Agoueve is not suitable for seed production), and an
additional 2 ha of land would be set aside for a nursery. The seed orchards
would be either owned by Government or leased from growers. A coconut seed
production officer would be appointed by the project after training for 3
months at the-Port Bouet station in Ivory Coast. Irrigation would be provided
by shallow wells. Artificial pollination would be carried out with imported
'Tall" pollen secured from Ivory Coast for which arrangements would be made
through IRHO. After germination the seeds would be transplanted in plastic
bags in the nursery, in a triangular pattern 60 cm apart (33,000 seeds per ha)
and watered regularly by hand. The seedlings would be ready for delivery to
growers after some six months, when about 80 cm tall.
10.       Seed Production Costs.  The following costs are based on a 4 ha seed
garden and a 2 ha nursery to produce 46,000 plants per year:
