Observations of Dwarf and Hybrid Groves
11.       The performance of existing groves of dwarfs and hybrids (para
4) would be monitored by SORAD at the following locations: Brenner plantation,
Agbodankope cooperative Atati Agbodan, and the Baguida plantation. Observ-
ations at Baguida would concern tall Malaysia trees. The program would
be carried out over five years.
12.       The observations would consist of (a) recording the number of
trees (living, dead, replacements) according to origin, (b) record the inter-
cropping system with food crops, (c) assigning a letter to each row working
from Wiest to East, (d) assigning a number to each tree in each line from South
to North; (e) harvesting each month and record the number of nuts per tree;
(f) noting the development of disease at each harvest, and (g) determining the
copra content twice a year. This work would require the assignment of a field
assistant to each of the plantations. The consultant in charge of the super-
vision of seed production would also supervise the program of observations.
Comparative Hybrid Trial
13.       In order to definitely  ascertain the performance of hybrids and
their resistance to the Kaincope disease, the Project would conduct a compara-
tive trial. The following 8 hybrids with yellow or Cameroon red dwarf (YD or
RD) would be compared: YD x West African Tall; YD x Polynesian Tall; YD
x Malaysia Tall; YD x Rennell Tall; RD x West African Tall; RD x Polynesia
Tall; RDx Malaysia Tall; and RD x Rennell Tall. The seedlings would be
purchased from Benin or Ivory Coast through IRHO.



ANNEX 9
Page 5
14.       According to the design prepared by IRHO during project prepara-
tion, and detailed in the feasibility study, the trial would be carried
out in a randomized block with 8 replications, with individual plots
planted to 25 trees each. With a density of 160 trees/ha, the trial would
cover 8 ha. The seedlings would be irrigated during the dry season for the
first two years. Annual records would be made of growth, foliar analysis,
and yields at the bearing stage. A technician selected by the coconut spe-
cialist of SORAD would be assigned to the trial. He would receive technical
support by the IRHO consultant. The trial would be conducted in liaison with
similar trials in neighboring countries (Ghana, Cameroon) carried out with
IRHO assistance.

TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
RENOVATION OF COCONUT PLANTINGS
Varietal Resistance of Coconuts to Kaincope 1/
1.        In Togo, 42 coconut trees of the "Malaysia Tall" type, planted in
1959 at Baguida, are free from the disease, while the "West Africa Tall"
coconuts are dying in the same area.  Similarly in Togo, dwarfs planted
between 1961 and 1963 (Malaysia Yellow Dwarfs, Cameroon Red Dwarfs, Brazil
Green Dwarfs) in areas devastated by the disease show only two cases of
death after 12 years, which seem to demonstrate a better resistance of
these types.
2.        A trial with dwarf coconuts vas carried out near Cape Saint Paul
in Ghana, including Malaysia Dwarfs in 1957, and Malaysia Dwarfs and Cameroon
Dwarfs imported from Ivory Coast and from Jamaica in 1966-67.  Nearly all
have died and their death was said to have resulted from the disease. Their
death however could be attributed to the unfavorable environnent in the Cape
St. Paul area (poor soil, inadequate rainfall, some 850 mm which means a
deficit of 800-1,000 mm); these conditions are detrimental to the development
of Dwarf coconut, which is more sensitive to drought than Tall. Under such
conditions, and keeping in mind the limited number of trees (8 to 14 in the
1966-67 trial), it is difficult to assess the resistance to the disease from
these results. In Cameroon an outbreak of the disease was observed in 1975
on Red Dwarfs, about 10 years old, at Ebome. In Jamaica, the Malaysia Dwarf
is resistant to the disease (4% mortality), in contrast to Jamaica Tall (85%
mortality). Malaysia, Thailand, and Cambodia Talls are less resistant (20
to 30% mortality), and the Ceylon and Panama Dwarfs are susceptible. The
hybrid Panama Tall x Malaysia Dwarf is tolerant (13%) and the Malaysia Dwarf x
Jamaica Tall is somewhat less tolerant (22%). These results were obtained
in an environment that is very favorable to the disease and where the Jamaica
Tall "factor" (high susceptibility) is a determining cause of the high
susceptibility.
3.        The tolerance of the last two hybrids, for whlich one of the parents
is susceptible to the disease, shows that it is possible to develop a tolerant
hybrid from susceptible types crossed with resistant types. Even though no
systematic trial has been conducted in Togo on the Yellow Dwarf x West African
Tall hybrid, there is reason to speculate that this hybrid is tolerant to the
Kaincope disease.
1/   From a note prepared following a meeting of representatives of the
World Bank, SODEPALM and IRHO on January 9, 1976 in Ivory Coast.

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
INFRASTRUCTURE
A. Roads
1.        The project area has a primary network of 245 Km of asphalt roads
linking the principal towns to Lome. In the western part, the asphalt road
to Palime runs through Keve connecting it with Lome. In the central part,
an asphalt road runs north-south across the entire project area connecting
Tsevie with Lome. Another asphalt road goes from Tsevie via Tabligbo, Vogan,
Aneho to Lome (Map 11,885). The remaining network within the project area
consists of 520 Km of secondary roads and 1,500 Km of feeder roads.
2.        Not all existing roads in the project area are adequate.  While
the primary roads are by and large adequate, the secondary and feeder roads
connecting villages and farms with these main roads are not. These links
are dirt roads and tracks which are frequently impassable in the rainy sea-
son, and often alsc in the dry season due to lack of maintenance. Even with
maintenance, many of them are inadequate. Many areas where rice production
would be developed and settlement encouraged remained inaccessible.
3.        The project road program would be carried out over PY 2-4 and
would encompass 300 km, namely: upgrading 90 km of feeder to secondary
roads, constructing 90 Km of new feeder roads, and rehabilitating 120 km
of existing feeder roads. SORAD would also be equipped to maintain this
network, as well as existing roads.
4.        Physical features of the area are favorable to road building.
The Maritime region occupies a broad rolling terrain which slopes gently
(0.2-0.3%) toward the coast. Generally, soils vary from loamy sand near
the coast to sandy loam and sandy clay in the valley bottomlands and to
lateritic on the uplands. Except in the bottomlands, the type of soil
could reduce topsoil stripping requirements.
5.        Streams are small and sparsely distributed.  Surface drainage
is not a serious problem, except in the valleys. In the sandy coastal
area, the problem is even less since the terrain is flat and percolation
is high. Under these conditions requirements for drainage structures such
as bridges and culverts would be moderate.
6.        Secondary roads would be 5 m wide, with an a verage 30 cm embank-
ment and capped by 15 cm of laterite; on average, there would be one culvert
per km. Feeder roads would be 3 m wide, with 10 cm of laterite, also with
one culvert per km.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
7.        In accordance with prevailing arrangements in the country, SORAD's
Rural Engineering Unit (REU) would in addition to annual maintenance, be
responsible for the construction or improvement of feeder roads; while up-
grading feeder to secondary roads would be done by private contractor.
Responsibility of the Public Works Department is restricted to main roads.
SORAD which has experience with feeder roads, but lacks equipment, would
identify priorities, prepare a plan of operation, and supervise construc-
tion. In order to carry out its task, the REU would be strengthened.
An engineer would be recruited to head the unit, while the associate en-
gineer--a relatively junior officer--currently in charge would remain as
deputy. Design staff would also be increased and maintenance/construction
equipment would be provided (details at Table 1).
8.        Construction costs for new roads are estimated at about CFAF 1.0
million per km of feeder on force account and at about CFAF 4.5 million per
km of new secondary road by contract (Table 2). The cost of rehabilitating
or upgrading existing roads is estimated at 60% of the cost of new construc-
tion, while direct annual maintenance costs (for materials, supplies, and
labor) is estimated 2% of construction cost. Detailed cost estimates are at
Table 2.
B. Village Water Supplies
9.        As the water table beyond the coastal sands is deep, the digging
of traditional wells is difficult. This lack of reliable water supply is a
major concern throughout the project area. The Government has supported the
construction of deep tubewells, equipped with pumps, each to serve several
villages, with operating costs being borne by the local administrative dis-
tricts (circonscriptions). However, the problem remains particularly acute
in certain parts of the project area--the savannah zone and the northern part
of the coastal plateau--where it presents a major obstacle to the expansion
of agricultural activity. In these areas, the water table is not only deep
but irregular. Villagers are beginning to install raîn water catchments via
metal roofs, gutters and cisterns, but this solution does not always meet
water needs during the long dry season (November to March). Where the
topography is suitable, substantial benefits could be obtained through
the construction of small retention dams which would catch the natural
runoff and serve as simple reservoirs.
10.       Provisions have been made under the project for the construction
and equipping of 20 wells and two retention dams; however, the mix may shift,
following the detailed examination of sites to be served and the possibili-
ties for each type of construction. Also where appropriate, dispensaries
and markets (paras 15 and 18) would be equipped with cisterns. Operation
and maintenance of the wells would be the responsibility of the "circon-
scriptions" who would therefore, have to agree on their construction, and
on their operation and maintenance thereafter.



ANNEX 10
Page 3
ll.       Tubewells would have an average depth of 65 m and would be equipped
with submersible electqic pumps powered by diesel generators. They would
discharge into a 300 m capacity concrete water tank which would have faucets
for delivering water to villagers.  Dams are expected to be 6-10 ! high, de-
en in§ o   ooraphK  with a stora  a                  aba            m    Water
tould re delerei tnt_oug  a ppe-an- a ce  stystem locutea oeaoou  I50 m
downstream from the dam. Based on average per capita consumption of 20
gallons per day, each well or dam is expected to serve a population of
2,000 people. Further technical details are at Appendices 1 (for wells)
and 2 (dams).
12.       Construction of the tubewells would be carried out by the Ministry
of Public Works' well brigade, which is adequately equipped. SORAD would
prepare a plan of operation, in consultation with the Office of Geology
and Mines of the Ministry of Public Works (which is responsible for the
hydrogeologic aspects of the well program), which would specify the prior-
ity location of the wells, taking into account the adequacy of the aquifer,
spacing of wells, and the population to be served. Dams would be constructed
on force account by the SORAD's Rural Engineering Unit after detailed surveys
and design by the Rural Engineering Department of MER. Both engineering
design and construction program for wells and dams would be submitted to
IDA for approval.
13.       Construction and equipment costs are estimated at about CFAF 16
million per well (CFAF 8,000 or US$36 per capita of population expected to
be served) and CFAF 10 million per retention dam. Annual operating and
maintenance costs are estimated at about CFAF 650,000 per well and at
about CFAF 200,000 per dam. Details are at Tables 3 and 4. Once con-
structed, the facilities would be taken over by the local administration
in line with prevailing arrangements in the project area for responsibility
for village water supply. Under these arrangements there is no water charge,
and operating costs are financed out of the local budget.
C. Dispensaries
14.       Like most of West Africa, the Maritime Region of Togo suffers high
rates of morbidity and mortality from diseases against which relatively simple
health measures can be extremely effective. Government's strategy of health
care involves the integration of major hospitals with primary health centers
and dispensaries. The dispensaries are designed to serve a population of
10,000 people or a radius of 7 km. This relatively broad coverage justifies
the level of staffing which consists of a nurse, an auxiliary midwife, and one
or more health assistants who ensure regular coverage of the surrounding vil-
lages, giving simple care and referring more serious cases to the health center.
15.       Health coverage of the southern Maritime Region is generally ade-
quate, but falls off sharply towards the north. The project would provide
for the construction, under SORAD supervision of 15 dispensaries. Each dis-
pensary would consist of a delivery room, recovery room, treatment rooms and



ANNEX 10
Page 4
2
two offices, covering a total of about 225 m , and would be equipped with
cisterns, sanitary facilities, refrigerator, and stove, plus an initial
stock of medicine and supplies. Costs, including equipment and supplies,
are estimated at CFAF 4 million per dispensary.
16.       Nurses would receive three years training at the Paramedical
School in Lome, while auxiliary midwives would receive one year of training
at the school at SOKODE. Health assistants would also receive one year of
training, primary on the job. Phasing of dispensary construction would be
coordinated with the availability of trained personnel. The Public Health
Service of the Ministry of Health would be responsible for the operation and
maintenance of the dispensaries once completed.
D. Markets
17.       Primary marketing, especially of food crops, is generally carried
out at weekly village markets, where buyers and sellers can meet. In order
to ensure competition between traders, and help stabilize the marketing of
primary commodities, the local authorities have begun to encourage an increase
in the number of villages able to hold regular markets through the provision
of sheltered areas. The project would assist this program by providing for
the construction of about 20 new markets. Ten sites have already been selec-
ted and the remainder would be determined in PY 3 after review of the results
of the program and the needs generated by increased production.
18.       The basic plan for covered rural markets has been developed by
Togo's Center for Construction and Housing and employs a modular system
using prefabricated materials. Each module, consisting of foundation, con-
crete drains and support posts, and aPuminium roof, contains eight market
stalls covering a total of about 95 m . Each markEt would contain four
shelters, with a total covered area of about 380 m , at an estimated cost
of CFAF 1.8 million per market.
19.       Construction would be carried out by contractors under SORAD super-
vision. Operation and maintenance of the markets would be the responsibility
of the district administration.

INFRASTRUCTURE
DETAILS OF DESIGN AND FEATURES OF TUBEWELLS
Geology and Hydrology
1.        Geologically, the Maritime Region is underlain by an impervious
basement complex of metamorphic rock (socle cristallin) extending from the
north (40-50 km inland). In the north, where most of the groundwater re-
charge comes from, the complex is shallow and exposed to the ground surface.
It gradually becomes deeper towards the south, reaching a depth of more
than 300 m near the coast. On top of this complex lie the aquifers in patchy
formations with depths varying from an average of 30 m for the uppS r aqui-
fers io more than 100 m for the lower ones, and yielding from 50 m /day up to
500 m /day depending on the extent and geologic formation Sf the aquifers. In
the vicinity of Lome the aquifer could yield up to 1,000 m /day.
Water-Demand Assumptions
2.        The maximum area served by a well is estimated to be with'n a ra-
dius of 1.5 km around the well.  Thus the area would be about 10 km , with
about 2,000 people expected to be the maximum population.
3.        Based on the peak consumption rate of 20 gallons per day per 3capita,
and 30% lossen, the total peak demand at the well would be about 200 m /day
or about 30 m /hr.
Water Tank Capacity
4.        The water tank of about 300 m  in capacity would be enough to meet
the demand in case the well requires minor repair for 1 to 2 days.
Pump Head and HP Required
5.        Based on the conservative specific capacity of the well of about
1 lit/sec/m, the well draw-down would be about 8 m. The average depth of the
aquifer is about 30 m; thus the total hydraulic head on the well pump includ-
ing losses would be about 41 m.

Page 2
6.        Under the above conditions, the pump needs 10 HP to lift the water.

INFRASTRUCTURE
DETAILED FEATURES OF A SMALL DAM
Topography and Hydrology
1.        The rolling terrain, the stream pattern, and earth materials in
the project area, particularly in the north, indicate a potential for small
reservoir development2  The annual rainfall, on average, of 1,000 mm over
a catchment of 3-5 km  could produce enough surface runof  for the proposed
reservoir, which would have a capacity of about 100,000 m    Allowing for
sedimentition and evaporation, the reservoir is expected to supply up to
70,000 m per year. On the basis of the peak demand of up to 20 gallons per
day per capita and 30% losses, the reservoir would be capable of serving
about 2,000 people.
Features of Dam and Reservoir
(i)  Types of dam       -       earth fill
(il)  Height             -       6-10 m
(iii)  Free board         -       1.5 m    3
(iv)  Total Capacity    -        100,000
(v)  Sediment Space    -        20,000 m
(vi)  Spillway - free overSlow with concrete gurfacing capable
of disch4rging 1.5 m per second per km of catchment.
For 4 km  of catchment, the spillwa   would be 20 m wide
with 0.30 afflux, discharging 6.0 m /sec.
(vii) The dam embankment would be earth-fill with clay core, if
necessary, having 4 m top width and 1:2 to 1:3 side slope with
rip-rap on the upstream face containing about 8,500 m of
fill.
(viii)  The foundation would be treated to the impervious zone.
Core wall would be sunk to an impervious foundation.
(ix)  The outlet work would consist of the outlet pipe of 6
inches in diameter covered with concrete, equipped with
a strainer on upstream side, and a gate valve on the
downstream.

(x)  The dam design would depend on the soil tests at the
proposed site.
Distribution System
(xi)  Main gravity pipeline - PVC pipe 6 inches in diameter - would

outlet gate valve. The branch pipeline - PVC pipe 4 inches
in diameter - would convey water from the main pipe to two
separate delivery points at a distance of about 25 m from the
main for villagers.
(xi!) The village water points would be installed with an adequate
concrete platform and faucets for villagers' consumption.

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
IRRIGATION ENGINEERING STUDY
Background
1.        In the lower Zio, Haho and Mono valleys, the rivers develop many
overflow channels in their broad flood plains. Development of irrigated
agriculture on these flood plains (about 15,000 ha) is hydrologically and
physically feasible. The potential for irrigation by pumping for double
cropping is limited by the low minimum flow of the dry season; and large-
scale irrigation by gravity for double cropping requires the construction
of important storage works (Annex 3); in either case heavy investments are
required per unit area of irrigated land. Such major investments are anti-
cipated for the Zio and Mono valleys, but not before the 1980s. Meanwhile,
there is scope for the low cost development of gravity irrigation for single
cropping during the flood season.
2.        From a study of the available hydrological, soil and topographic
(1:50,000 scale) data, and field visits, the appraisal mission identified a
promising such scheme in the lower Zio valley between Djagble and Devego,
about 15 km northeast of Lome (map 11866). The topography is such (map
12155) that a main canal could be built at minimum expense, and the require-
ments for a flood protection level would be minimal. Moreover, the scheme
would not interfere with the possible expansion of the irrigation developed
upstream with Chinese assistance, and with the presence of oil palm or coco-
nut plantations as can be found upstream. The area is free from salinity,
and land tenure does not appear to present a serious obstacle. With the aid
of a 1:10,000-scale map which covers only part of the area, it has been
estimated that about 1,000 ha (net) could be served by the scheme, and that
the scheme (civil works) would cost in the neighborhood of CFAF 350.0 million
(US$1.5 million). Rice and vegetables could be expected to be grown with
120% cropping intensity.
Proposed Engineering Study
3.        Because of the reconnaissance nature of its identification, the
study would be carried out in two stages - feasibility and final design. The
basic data would first be developed. If the feasibility is confirmed, then
the planning and design phase would be carried out.
4.        The feasibility study would include the following surveys:
(a) Water Resources
i.   Installing staff gauges in the Zio River at two different
locations - Djagble and Devego (see map), making sure that



ANNEX 11
Page 2
each gauge would measure the lowest water level during
the dry season and the highest flood stage during the
flood season.
ii.   Observing daily water levels at these two locations by
reading the water level once a day at the installed
staff gauges. The governnent responsible agency must
send its officer to inspect and collect the water level
records from the gauge readers every Friday during the
period from June to October, and on the lst of each month
during November to May. The original of the records would
be kept at the Lome office ready for review.
(b) Topography
iii.   Preparing 1:10,000 scale topographic maps of the proposed
project area, which is located on the eastern bank of the
Zio river between Djagble and Devego (see map) showing
contour lines at 1.00 m intervals and intermediate concour
lines at 0.50 m intervals wherever land features are ir-
regular.
iv.   Making cross-sections of the Zio river between Djagble and
Devego at every 500 m interval along the river.
(c) Reference Datum
v.   To relate the gauges, cross-sections and contour lines to
the same datum, a permanent benchmark would be installed
at the Djagble for reference.
(d)  Soil Survey
vi.   Preparing a detailed soil survey and, if possible, land
classification (U.S. Bureau of Reclamation Standard) of
the project soil on the 1:10,000 scale maps.
(e) Water Quality
vii.   Sampling the Zio river water at Djagble for determining
salinity, sodium hazard and pH (preferably following the
U.S. Department of Agriculture Standard). Samples should
be taken in June, August, October and February.
(f)  Land Tenure
viii.   Surveying a land tenure of the project area showing the
size distribution of the holding, preparing a land con-
solidation plan and drawing up a settlement program to be
ready for review before the final stage of the project
feasibility study preparation.



ANNEX 11
Page 3
The data would be analyzed and reported, together with the conclusions and
recommendations of the feasibility study, in a report to be submitted to
Government and IDA.
5.        The engineering design of the gravity irrigation system would be
carried out if the conclusions of the feasibility study are favorable and
Government decides to proceed with the irrigation scheme. The work would
consist of (a) the design of project structures (diversion weirs, outlet
works, checks, canals, dikes and drains); (b) an evaluation of required
inputs and costs; (c) the.preparation of bidding documents, tendering and
bid evaluation; (d) needs for infrastructure; and (e) an economic analysis
of the irrigation projects in liaison with the agricultural development
study.
6.        The agricultural development study would be conducted concurrently
with the engineering design study. It would include (a) evaluation of the
agronomic potential of the soils of the project area; (b) selection of a
suitable cropping pattern, crop varieties, rotations, input and labor re-
quirements; (c) possibilities for vegetable development, second cropping
after irrigated rice; (d) estimates of yields and returns; (e) determina-
tion of farm size; (f) estimates of required support services, their orga-
nization and management; (g) preparation of a settlement scheme, including
land tenure aspects; (h) estimates of costs and returns, and (i) preparation
of a request for financing.
7.        Implementation.  The study would be under the responsibility of
SORAD, with consultant support. SORAD would coordinate the work with all
other agencies. A major role would be played by the Technical Division of
Rural Engineering Department (Genie Rural) of the Ministry of Rural Equip-
ment. The Division has six engineers, 10 technicians and five draftsmen.
An important role of the consultants would be to train the personnel of
the Rural Engineering Department. The consultants would have access to
other agencies, which would provide some basic information and facilities.
The principal of them are: (a) the Public Works Department; (b) the Mete-
orological Services; (c) the Hydrological Services (ORSTOM) which would
carry out the water resource and quality studies; (d) the soil survey ser-
vice of MDR which would carry out the detailed soil survey; (e) the topo-
graphic service which would carry out the topographic survey and be respon-
sible for the installation of a benchmark; (f) SORAD which would be respon-
sible for the coordination of the engineering study, the preparation of the
agricultural development phase, and the request for financing; (g) the rural
engineering department of MDR which would be responsible for the design work;
and (h) RMWA personnel which would assist with the preparation of the irriga-
tion project.
8.        Consultants.  An experienced irrigation engineer would be engaged
for a total of about one year. If within the first 6 months his conclusions
are positive and acceptable to Government and to IDA, he would be retained
for another period of about 6 months for carrying on the design phase with
the help of a design engineer who would be engaged six months. Qualifica-
tions of the consultants are given in Appendix 1.



ANNEX il
Page 4
9.        Costs.  Estimated total expenditures for the engineering study
would be about CFAF 18.0 million (US$80,000) - about 5% of the project civil
work cost - about 70% of which - CFAF 12.4 million (US$55,000) - would be
the foreign exchange component, which includes all technical assistance
costs (Table 1).



ANNEX 11
Appendix 1
Page 1
TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
IRRIGATION ENGINEERING STUDY
TERMS OF REFERENCE OF CONSULTANTS
1.        Irrigation Engineer.  SORAD would engage an irrigation engineer for
an initial period of 6 months to be extended for another 6 months if the design
phase is carried out. The engineer would assist in the preparation of the en-
gineering study of the irrigation project in the Lower Zio Deltaic Plain. He
would report to the Project Manager.  His responsibilities would be:
i.   Reviewing the existing basic data, staffing requirements and
facilities for the purposes of administering and executing the
project feasibility study, and coordinating with participating
agencies.
ii. Preparing the study work program to include that for additional
investigations and data procurement deemed necessary for both
technical and economic analyses, and advising on staffing and
facilities.
iii.   Implementing the work programs which would include the additional
investigations and data procurement, advising on the specifica-
tions and methods in carrying out these investigations and
procurement.
iv.   Interpreting the data obtained from the investigations and
procurement as above and preparing an interim report to
include recommendations and steps required for the completion
of final feasibility study preparation, should the data indi-
cate the project viability.
v.   Preparing the feasibility study work program, and arrangements
for staffing and facilities as necessary for implementing the
program.
vi.   Directing detailed field surveys of the diversion heading,
main canals, dikes and drains to the extent adequate for
costing.
vii.   Planning and designing of the irrigation and drainage structures.
viii.   Advising and assisting in planning agricultural development and
in analyzing the project economic aspect.



ANNEX 11
Appendix 1
Page 2
ix.   Advising on the organization matters in respect of the
project implementation, operation and maintenance.
x.   Advising and assisting in training of staff as well as
on the research matters relevant to the project prepara-
tion and implementation.
xi.   Preparing the project feasibility study reports.
xii.   Preparing tender documents.
The irrigation Engineer should have good background in hydrology. It is
required that he has at least 5 years experience in planning surface water
development, particularly for the river-run diversion irrigation scheme,
flood protection and drainage.  He should personally have conducted field
investigations, project planning and design and must have good organiza-
tional and training abilities. In particular, he should be familiar with
on-farm irrigation and with project economic evaluation.
2.        Assistant Design Engineer.  He would be a hydraulic structural
design engineer with at least 3 years practical experience in designing
small diversion weirs, outlet works, checks, canals, dikes and drains.
He would assist the Irrigation Engineer in designing the project struc-
tures and therefrom quantify the project civil work costs, the materials
supplied and labor needed. He would also participate in training the local
staff.

ANNEX 12
TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
REPORTING REQUIREMENTS
1.        The lack of a firm agricultural data base in the project area, and
the fact that the project, which involves rural development, includes several
elements whose final impact cannot be completely predicted, make it necessary
to firm up the data base, and to carefully measure the performance of the
project. To this end, the project authority and the evaluation unit will
keep comprehensive yearly records on performance and measure accomplishments
against appraisal estimates.
2.        To provide an effective tool to ensure a comprehensive recording
of project progress on all important aspects at the end of each cropping
year, a set of detailed tables (1 to 13) have been prepared and would be
discussed with to the Project Manager during the first supervision mission.
For each item the tables include appraisal estimates, the actual data
in absolute terms, and the actual data in relation to appraisal estimates.
4.        In addition to the yearly report, the Project would submit quarterly
reports on expenditures, disbursement, program of work, and implementation.
The format of these reports would be prepared and discussed with the project
authority during the first supervision mission.

ANNEX 13
Page 1
TOGO
MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR INTERNATIONALLY RECRUITED STAFF AND COUNTERPARTS
Detailed Terms of Reference will be issued by the Project Manager,
Technical Director
1.        The Technical Director would report to the Project Manager.  He
would be responsible in particular for:
(a) planning the project's program of work and of its budget
in liaison with the Financial and Administrative Manager;
(b) reviewing and establishing all technical recommendations
for project activities;
(c) coordinating all technical activities, including the
implementation of the infrastructure program;
(d) coordinating day-to-day activities with other Government
agencies operating in the project area;
(e) supervising all technical personnel, specially at the
senior level;
(f) preparing quarterly reports of activities;
(g) preparing tenders and contracts with the help of the
Financial and Administrative Manager; and
(h) training a successor.
He would be appointed for a five-year term.  He would have a degree in agri-
culture or equivalent scientific qualification and have experience in agri-
culture, preferably for five years in a senior supervisory position in a
Government extension service or equivalent, in West Africa, or under con-
ditions similar to those of West Africa.
Training Division Chief
2.        The Training Division Chief would report to the Technical Director,
He would be responsible for:



ANNEX 13
Page 2
(a) assisting with the evaluation and selection of candi-
dates to project posts;
(b)  organizing courses for new project staff at all levels
and refresher courses for project personnel (crop pro-
duction, extension techniques);
(c) training his counterpart;
(d)   supervising in-service training;
(e) coordinating training activities with the Ministry
of Rural Development and linking with IITA;
(f) supervising preparation of extension pamphlets,
brochures and posters; and
(g) training leaders and accountants of grower asso-
ciations.
It is expected that an internationally recruited specialist will
be needed for a period not exceeding three years. He would have an appro-
priate basic degree preferably with an additional qualification in education.
He should have experience in agriculture, preferably including a supervisory
position in a training school in West Africa or under conditions similar to
those of West Africa. His Togolese counterpart should have a similar degree
from a Togolese or foreign institution, and must be able to take over as
Training Division Chief within three years.
Financial and Administrative Manager
3.        The Financial and Administrative Manager would report to the Proj-
ect Manager.  He would be responsible for:
A.   Finances
(a) supervising all project financial administration;
(b) preparing annual budgets, in liaison with the Tech-
nical Directors;
(c)  preparing bi-yearly cash flow projections;
(d)  participating in the preparation of quarterly
reports;
(e) checking accounts and preparing final accounts; and
(f) preparing reimbursement claims relative to the IDA
Credit.



ANNEX 13
Page 3
B.   Procurement
(a) controlling the Project's supply activities, especially the
lists of input needs, and the coordination of orders and
deliveries; and
(b) participation in the preparation of tenders and contracts.
C.   Credit
Controlling inventory accountancy.
D.   Personnel
Administering personnel files under the control of the Project
Manager.
It is expected that an internationally recruited specialist would
have to be recruited; he would be appointed for not more than four years.
He would have an appropriate qualification in administration and finances
and would preferably have five years experience in a senior supervisory
position in West Africa or under conditions similar to those of West Africa.
His Togolese counterpart would have similar qualifications in accounting and
finances, and should be in a position to take up full responsibility as
Financial and Administrative Manager within four years.
Research Specialist
4.        The Research Specialist would report to the Technical Director for
administrative matters and work in close liaison with the Director of IRAT-
Togo. He would be responsible for:
(a) designing and carrying out a program of field experiments
according to the general outline prepared jointly by the
Director of IRAT-Togo and the Technical Director and linked
with the IITA regional program;
(b) training research assistants, and his counterpart;
(c) ensuring the coordination with the other research activities
in the Project area, and with regional programs in West
Africa in liaison with IITA;
(d) preparing technical recommendations that are acceptable and
profitable to farmers, and participate in the extension
program;



ANNEX 13
Page 4
(e)  supervising the installation of the coconut trials; and
(f) providing technical support to the vegetable testing program.
It is expected that an expatriate would be needed and would be
appointed for a maximum of three years. He would have a degree in agronomy
and have experience in the design and implementation of field research,
preferably in countries with conditions simiLar to those of West Africa.
A background in soil management and/or maize management would be desirable.
He should be able to:
(a) work with Togolese counterparts and train them;
(b) communicate both with French and English speaking research
workers; and
(c) translate research data into locally feasible and economically
viable packages of improved practices.
His Togolese counterpart would have,a degree in agronomy from a
Togolese or foreign institution, and should show a potential for assuming
research responsibilities after three years or less of involvement in the
program.
Director of CNCA Project Credit Agency (PCA)
5.        The project Credit Unit Director would be a senior agricultural
Credit specialist, fully conversant with accounting procedures and with
administrative, technical and financial organization of rural credit insti-
tutions. Long experience in responsible positions in smallholder rural
credit and administration in developing countries with conditions similar
to those of Western Africa would be required. Qualifications as agricul-
turalist, experience in the formation of farmers' credit groups or societies,
and working with these organizations or cooperatives would be advantages.
Fluent French is essential. His prime responsibilities would be the control
of project-farmers' group credit operations which would involve initially
the disbursement of loans in kind and their recovery when due.
His responsibilities would include:
(a) PCA administrative, technical and financial organization;
(b) establishing an efficient accounting and recording system
so as to be in permanent control of borrowers' position in
principal and interest, by category of loans, due date and
farm location;
(c)  organizing the procedure for receiving, registering, filing
farmers' credit applications;



ANNEX 13
Page 5
(d)  in consultation with the Project field extension officers
as regards the Project Technical criteria, establishing the
procedure for loan evaluation, loan supervision and loan
collection;
(e) establishing draft documents covering all aspects of credit
and accounting procedures and administration;
(f) in liaison with the Project Supply Ùnit and Project field
extension officers requisitioning of inputs and farm equipment
for timely delivery to farmers;
(g)  preparation of a credit manual to be used by Project field
Officers and PCA credit assistants;
(h) training of credit staff;
(i) in service training of his deputy on all technical and
financial aspects of credit operations, so as to enable him
to take over efficiently the management of the Credit Agencies
the soonest possible.
The PCA Director would be administratively responsible to CNCA.
Chief - Monitoring Unit
6.        The chief of the Project Monitoring Unit would vork in close
liaison with the Technical Director and the Head of the Credit Agency.
He would assist vith:
(a)  designing a detailed program of project monitoring both at the
farm and at the project level, including sampling procedures,
yield determination, design of checklists and questionnaires,
and data processing methods;
(b)  training the office and field personnel of the monitoring unit
in sampling, farm surveying and data processing methods;
(c) gathering and analyzing data available from other project
activities, securing additional data if needed, and monitoring
farmers' attitudes towards innovation introduced by the project;
(d) reporting on the progress of the project to Government authorities
and to IDA; and
(e) advising on possible re-orientation of certain aspects of the
project.



ANNEX 13
Page 6
The chief of the Project Monitoring Unit would be an individual capable of
giving independent judgement. He would be an agricultural economist with
experience in farm surveys and statistics in the tropics.

ANNEX 18

MARITIME REGION RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ECONOMIC RATE OF RETURN
1.        Economic costs and benefits are shown in Table 1 for each component
of the project. Benefits have been calculated using the prices detailed in
Annex 15. All costs include physical contingencies, but are net of taxes
and price contingencies. On farm costs include land preparation and mills
for the rice component, improvements to the station at Agoueve for vegetables,
and equipment for all components. Incremental family labor is costed at the
going agricultural wage rate of CFAF 100 per man-day, except for that required
to water young coconut trees during the dry season (when no other agricultural
opportunities are present) which is evaluated at CFAF 50 per man-day. Field
staff costs include the salaries and equipment (bicycles or motorcycles) for
extension workers and subsector chiefs.
2.        All other administrative costs (including those of the Credit Agency
and Input Supply Unit) have been grouped under general administration. These
costs have been apportioned to the various project components on the basis of
their relative shares of total field staff costs.
3.        Infrastructure costs include road maintenance (equipment, salaries,
and operating costs of the Rural Engineering Unit), construction and improve-
ment of feeder roads, village stores, and markets. These costs have been
apportioned to various components on the basis of the number of farmers, i.e.
among the coastal plateau, depleted coastal plateau, savannah, and rice com-
ponents. No infrastructure improvement is planned in the coconut zone and
the vegetable farmers would only be incidental beneficiaries of developments
justified on the basis of the larger components. Secondary roads, whose
enefits would not be fully captured by the project, have not been included
in the calculation. No attempt has been made to quantify the benefits of
dispensaries and village water supplies, and their costs have been omitted
from the calculations.
4.        The rate of return of the coconut component was calculated over a
project life of 30 years, the expected life of trees. Project life for all
other components has been estimated at 15 years, on the assumption that bene-
fits could be maintained at the level obtained in PY 5 for an additional
10 years.
5.        On the basis of the above, the overall rate of return of the pro-
ject is 37%. The rates for the major component range from 13% for coconuts
to 52% for vegetables. Results of a sensitivity analysis are given in
Table 2. This shows that most project actions could stand substantial
adverse developments, such as increases in costs or lower expected benefits
and remain economic. The exception would be activities on the depleted
coastal plateau, the viability of which would be seriously threatened by
a decrease in benefits.



ANNEX 18
Page 2
6.        In the case of maize, rice and cotton, the economic prices calcu-
lated for input substitution or export value differ substantially from the
average domestic market prices used for the farm budgets. To illustrate the
importance of economic pricing, the benefits of the coastal plateau, depleted
coastal plateau, savannah, and rice components were re-estimated using the
domestic market prices for these commodities. The major change would be to
reduce the savannah component to 5%.
7.        The coconut component has an expected return of 13% on the assump-
tion of a 30-year life. Because the resistance of the hybrids to Kaincope
disease has not been certified through scientific tests, it could be argued
the average life of the trees to be planted should be reduced to the current
age of existing hybrids (planted in early sixties) which as yet show no signs
of infection. On this basis, project life would be 18 years, yielding a rate
of return of 6%. However, this is an extremely conservative assumption and,
given the fact that there currently is no productive alternative for the
coconut zone, the risk is acceptable.
8.        Reducing the project life of all other components from 15 years to
10 years would yield an overall rate of return of 32%.
9.        While the secondary roads are excluded from the above calculations,
they are expected to have a satisfactory economic justification. Based on a
separate study conducted for such roads throughout Togo, the secondary roads
would have rates of return between 18 and 25%.
